3:68:3525:0:28:71:175:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:1  Introduction
3:69:3540:28:16:71:534:680:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Healthcare spending is one of the largest scal challenges facing the U.S. federal government. In
3:70:3637:16:16:71:536:664:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2014, the U.S. federal government spent $1.1 trillion on public healthcareprograms (BEA, 2015)
3:71:3731:16:16:71:535:648:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and the CBO projects that spending will grow to $2.0 trillion by 2026 (CBO, 2016). Within the
3:72:3824:16:17:71:534:632:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:healthcare system, post-acute care (PAC) is an under-studied sector, with large stakes for both
3:73:3919:17:16:71:205:615:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:spending and patient health.
3:74:3947:16:16:88:534:599:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Post-acute care is the term used for formal care provided to help patients recover from a surgery
3:75:4044:16:16:71:537:583:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or some other acute care event. Medicare spending on PAC is substantial, about $60 billion in 2013,
3:76:4143:16:17:71:534:567:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or about 20% more than the much-studied Medicare Part D program. PAC spending isgrowing
3:77:4230:17:16:71:535:550:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:faster than overall Medicare spending. PAC spending accounts for almost three-quarters of the
3:78:4323:16:16:71:536:534:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:unexplained geographic variation in Medicare spending (Newhouse et al., 2013). The quality of
3:79:4416:16:16:71:537:518:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-acute care has potentially important consequences for patient health;over 40% of hospital
3:80:4510:16:17:71:535:502:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients are discharged to PAC, and PAC patients are high-risk, with 15% ofMedicare deaths
3:81:4600:17:16:71:278:485:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:involving a PAC stay in the prior 30 days.1
3:82:4643:16:16:88:535:469:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In this paper, we study the impact of provider nancial incentivesin determining patient ows
3:83:4736:16:16:71:537:453:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and government spending in the Medicare PAC system. The PAC setting isattractive for several
3:84:4828:16:17:71:534:437:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reasons.  First, given its scal importance, understanding the e¤ects of nancial incentives is a
3:85:4925:17:16:71:537:420:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:natural area for inquiry. Second, the institutional setting  involving multiple interlocking and po-
3:86:5026:16:16:71:535:404:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tentially substitutable settings that operate under di¤erent reimbursement regimes  suggests that
3:87:5124:16:16:71:535:388:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:nancial incentives may have rst order consequences. Third, ine¢ciencies in the PAC sector have
3:88:5220:16:17:71:534:372:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:potentially important implications for public health, given that PAC patients are disproportionately
3:89:5320:17:16:71:444:355:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:high-risk and might be more vulnerable to ine¢ciencies in the delivery of care.
3:90:5399:16:16:88:535:339:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our analysis focuses on patients whose point of entry into the PAC system is an LTCH.2We
3:91:5487:16:16:71:537:323:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:focus on LTCH patients because of the sharp variation in provider incentives at this type of facility.
3:92:5589:16:17:71:536:307:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This is illustrated in Figure 1: providers are reimbursed a daily amount (of approximately $1,300)
3:93:5687:17:16:71:535:290:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:up to a threshold number of days, at which point there is a large (approximately $13,000 on average)
3:94:5787:16:16:71:535:274:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increase in payments for keeping a patient an additional day beyond the threshold, but no payments
3:95:5885:16:17:71:535:258:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for any days beyond it. We investigate the e¤ects of this jump in payments at the threshold using
3:96:5984:17:16:71:534:241:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:detailed Medicare claims and administrative data on the universe of LTCH stays over the 2007-2012
3:97:6081:16:16:71:534:225:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:period, when this non-linear payment schedule was in e¤ect, as well asthe 2000-2002 period, when
3:98:6177:16:16:71:502:209:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs were instead reimbursed under a linear (i.e., constant per-diem) payment schedule.
3:99:6265:16:17:88:535:193:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We start by presenting descriptive evidence on the e¤ect of the jump in payments. Discharges
3:100:6357:17:16:71:530:176:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:respond strongly to the payment increase, with the share of stays discharged increasing from 2%
3:101:6452:16:16:71:535:160:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to 9% at precisely the day of the jump. The marginal patient discharged at the threshold appears
3:102:6548:16:18:71:536:144:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to be much healthier than the average dischargee prior to the threshold. At the threshold, pa-
3:103:6642:18:17:84:537:126:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:1These statistics are taken from MedPAC (2004), MedPAC (2015a), and MedPAC (2015b), with the exception of
3:104:6747:17:10:71:392:109:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:the statistic on deaths which we calculate using the data described in Section 2.
3:105:6828:10:17:84:536:99:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:2The acronym LTCH is typically pronounced el-tack, presumably reecting the fact that LTCHs are sometimes
3:106:6935:17:0:71:437:82:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:referred to as long-term acute care hospitals (LTACs), which is pronounced in this manner.
4:107:7026:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tients are disproportionately more likely to be discharged to a lessintensive PAC facility or home
4:108:7124:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(downstream) than to an acute care hospital (upstream); they also have substantially lower
4:109:7218:16:16:71:381:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-discharge mortality than patients discharged on earlier days.
4:110:7284:16:16:88:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:A natural question raised by this evidence is whether distortions inthe timing of discharge have
4:111:7380:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an impact on patient health. Given the high baseline mortality rate for LTCHpatients (30% die
4:112:7473:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:within 90 days of LTCH admission), if the distortions are harmful, it seems plausible that we could
4:113:7572:16:16:71:537:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:detect an e¤ect.  Empirical analysis is challenging, however, because unlike discharge behavior,
4:114:7668:16:17:71:345:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality e¤ects may not appear right at the threshold.
4:115:7725:17:16:88:536:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The available evidence shows no compelling evidence of any mortality e¤ects from the distortions
4:116:7821:16:16:72:534:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in discharge behavior. We nd no evidence of a change in the level or the slope of the mortality
4:117:7917:16:16:72:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hazard in the vicinity of the threshold. We provide an additional informative contrast by comparing
4:118:8016:16:17:72:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for-prot and non-prot LTCHs. We show that while both types of hospitals have similar payment
4:119:8110:17:16:72:534:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedules, the behavioral response in discharges is much stronger for for-prot hospitals. Despite
4:120:8208:16:16:72:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the larger behavioral response at the threshold, we nd no indicationof a mortality impact even
4:121:8303:16:16:72:196:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for the for-prot hospitals.
4:122:8331:16:17:88:532:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While these results provide no smoking gun evidence of patient harm, they do not allow
4:123:8419:17:16:72:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:us to comprehensively rule out a mortality e¤ect. We cannot, for instance, rule out an e¤ect for
4:124:8515:16:16:72:536:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:every type of patient or at each and every hospital, and we cannot rule outadverse health e¤ects
4:125:8611:16:16:72:535:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that would not manifest in higher mortality rates. However, at minimum,we do not detect robust
4:126:8705:16:17:72:535:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:enough mortality e¤ect that would allow us to use the jump in payments toestimate how LTCHs
4:127:8796:17:16:72:534:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:trade o¤ prots and patient health. Indeed, the results suggest that the marginal patients are able
4:128:8895:16:16:72:533:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to receive similar care whether they are located in an LTCH or in their alternative setting, which
4:129:8993:16:16:72:504:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:empirically is usually a less intensive PAC facility such as a Skilled Nursing Facility (SNF).
4:130:9087:16:17:88:536:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Another natural question raised by the descriptive evidence on discharge behavior at the thresh-
4:131:9183:17:16:72:537:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:old is how providers respond to the payment schedule on days further from the threshold. Likewise,
4:132:9281:16:16:72:537:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the descriptive analysis does not provide a natural way to gauge the magnitude of provider response,
4:133:9381:16:16:72:537:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or to estimate how treatment patterns and Medicare payments would be a¤ected by counterfactual
4:134:9475:16:17:72:161:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment schedules.
4:135:9493:17:16:88:536:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:To address these questions, we specify and estimate a dynamic model of LTCH discharge behav-
4:136:9585:16:16:72:536:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ior. In our model, patients are characterized by their health, which evolves stochastically over time.
4:137:9687:16:16:72:536:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs face a (daily) decision of whether to retain the patient or discharge her to another facility.
4:138:9787:16:17:72:534:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The LTCHs objective function includes both net revenue (Medicare payments net of costs) and
4:139:9879:17:16:72:536:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patient utility. If the patient is discharged from the LTCH, the provider receives zero net revenue,
4:140:9979:16:16:72:536:171:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but internalizes at least a portion of the patient utility from beingtreated in an alternative loca-
4:141:10079:16:17:72:536:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tion. If the LTCH keeps the patient, it receives net revenue that depends on Medicares payment
4:142:10174:17:16:72:535:138:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule, while also accounting for the patients utility from being treated in the LTCH and the
4:143:10270:16:16:72:535:122:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:option value of making a similar discharge decision the following day. The provider therefore faces
4:144:10369:16:16:72:282:106:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a standard dynamic discrete choice problem.
4:145:10412:16:17:88:534:90:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We estimate the model by simulated method of moments to match the observed discharge and
4:146:10500:17:0:72:535:73:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality patterns under the linear and non-linear payment schedules.To take advantage of the
5:147:10594:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:variation provided by the sharp jump in payments, we assign greater weights to moments that are
5:148:10689:17:16:71:401:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:close to the jump. The estimated model ts the data reasonably well.
5:149:10757:16:16:88:535:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use the model and the estimated parameters to investigate the e¤ects of alternative contracts
5:150:10853:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that  like the observed contract  have a daily reimbursement rate up to a cap but that  unlike
5:151:10950:16:17:71:537:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the observed contract  do not have a jump in payments at a threshold day.We nd, for example,
5:152:11044:17:16:71:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that if we were to lower the xed payment to eliminate the jump in payments at the threshold, we
5:153:11140:16:16:71:536:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:would reduce total payments per admission for the episode of care by 25% on average, or about
5:154:11233:16:17:71:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:$13,000 per admission. However, such a payment schedule substantially reduces LTCH revenue
5:155:11323:17:16:71:535:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and estimated prots, and therefore may have out-of-sample impacts on LTCH behavior that our
5:156:11415:16:16:71:456:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:estimates would not capture, such as induced LTCH exit or lower service quality.
5:157:11495:16:16:88:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We therefore also engage in a more conservative set of counterfactuals in which we restrict
5:158:11586:16:17:71:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:attention to alternative contracts that would hold the LTCH harmless iftheir behavior did not
5:159:11679:17:16:71:536:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:change. Specically, we consider the set of contracts that hold LTCHs revenue constant under their
5:160:11777:16:16:71:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:observed discharge schedule. Thus, if we apply this schedule and ittriggers behavioral response
5:161:11872:16:16:71:534:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by LTCHs then they must be better o¤. Using our estimated model, we are ableto identify a
5:162:11961:16:17:71:536:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:broad set of win-win payment schedules that reduce Medicare payments and, by construction,
5:163:12053:17:16:71:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:leave LTCHs (weakly) better o¤.3The contract that generates the largest savings reduces Medicare
5:164:12149:16:16:71:535:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments for the episode of care by 4.5%, and increases LTCH prots by 5.1%. Interestingly, this
5:165:12245:16:16:71:534:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reduction in Medicare payments is slightly larger than the reduction from changes in organization
5:166:12342:16:17:71:535:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:form; our model estimates imply that if all LTCHs behaved like non-prots rather than all LTCHs
5:167:12438:17:16:71:512:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:behaving like for-prots  Medicare payments per episode of care would be reduced by 2.9%.
5:168:12528:16:16:88:536:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our paper relates to a large literature examining how healthcare spending responds to nancial
5:169:12622:16:16:71:535:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:incentives.  Given the importance of healthcare spending in the economy and in public sector
5:170:12714:16:17:71:534:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:budgets, the existence of this large literature is not surprising.What is surprising  and arguably
5:171:12813:17:16:71:535:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:unfortunate from this perspective  is that the vast majority of thisliterature (including much of our
5:172:12915:16:16:71:536:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:own work) has focused on the impact ofconsumernancial incentives, such as deductibles and co-
5:173:13009:16:16:71:534:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments, with relatively less attention to the impact ofprovidernancial incentives.4The majority
5:174:13107:16:17:71:534:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of healthcare spending, however, is accounted for by a small share of high-cost individuals whose
5:175:13204:17:16:71:537:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:spending is largely in the catastrophic range where deductibles and co-payments no longer bind,
5:176:13301:16:16:71:530:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and thus consumer cost-sharing is likely to have little impact relativeto provider-side incentives.
5:177:13400:16:16:88:534:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The relative lack of research on the provider side presumably reects the di¢culties in nding
5:178:13494:16:17:71:535:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:clean variation in incentives to model and study. Perhaps not surprisingly, the sharp incentives
5:179:13590:17:18:71:533:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:created by the current LTCH payment schedule have already received some attention in both
5:180:13679:18:17:84:536:169:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:3Given the lack of compelling evidence of mortality e¤ects atthe threshold, it seems reasonable to assume that
5:181:13789:17:10:71:509:152:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:mortality impacts are also unlikely to be rst-order under these LTCH held harmless alternative contracts.
5:182:13897:10:17:84:536:142:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:4The literature on the impact of consumer incentives (moralhazard) in health insurance is too vast to try to
5:183:14007:17:13:72:536:125:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:summarize or cite here. Existing work on provider-side incentives has focused on descriptive evidence that providers
5:184:14123:13:13:72:537:112:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:do respond to incentives, with much of the evidence coming from the response to the introduction of the Inpatient
5:185:14236:13:13:72:537:99:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:Prospective Payment System in 1983 (Cutler and Zeckhauser (2000) provide a review of this evidence). More recently,
5:186:14351:13:0:72:522:86:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:Clemens and Gottlieb (2014) provide a rare look at the behavioral response of physicians to nancial incentives.
6:187:14464:0:17:71:533:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:academic (Kim et al., 2015) and popular (Weaver et al., 2015) spheres. Our descriptive work on
6:188:14558:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges around the threshold is quite similar to this prior work,while our descriptive analysis
6:189:14656:16:16:71:535:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the health of the marginal dischargee and our exploration of mortality e¤ects is new.  Our
6:190:14749:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:paper is most closely related to Eliason et al. (2016) who  in independentwork  also study the
6:191:14845:16:17:71:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:impact of the LTCH payment schedule on discharge behavior descriptively and through the lens of
6:192:14940:17:16:71:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a dynamic model. Where comparable, the results are broadly similar to ours;however they focus
6:193:15033:16:16:71:536:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:more on heterogeneity in provider responses and, unlike us, do not analyze patient mortality or
6:194:15128:16:17:71:211:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-discharge healthcare use.
6:195:15158:17:16:88:536:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, from a more conceptual perspective, our paper is related to a growing literature that
6:196:15252:16:16:71:536:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:seeks to interpret descriptive evidence of the behavioral responses to non-linear payment schedules
6:197:15351:16:16:71:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(bunching) through the lens of economic models that allow for assessments ofbehavior under
6:198:15443:16:17:71:526:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:counterfactual schedules (e.g., Chetty et al., 2011; Einav et al., 2015; Manoliand Weber, 2017).
6:199:15539:17:16:88:532:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The rest of the paper proceeds as follows. Section 2 provides some background on the PAC
6:200:15627:16:16:71:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:sector, LTCHs, and our data. In Section 3, we describe the discharge and mortality patterns around
6:201:15725:16:16:71:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the jump in payments. Section 4 presents the model, discusses estimation, and presents the results
6:202:15823:16:38:71:289:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from our counterfactuals. Section 5 concludes.
6:203:15869:38:28:71:205:426:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:2  Setting and data
6:204:15888:28:23:71:311:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:2.1  Post-acute care in the United States
6:205:15929:23:16:71:535:375:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Post-acute care (PAC) is the term for rehabilitation and palliative services provided to patients
6:206:16026:16:17:71:533:359:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:recovering from an acute care hospital stay. In the United States, theCenter for Medicaid and
6:207:16119:17:16:71:535:342:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare Services (CMS) associates PAC with three types of facilities  long-term care hospitals
6:208:16215:16:16:71:535:326:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(LTCHs), skilled nursing facilities (SNFs), and inpatient rehabilitation facilities (IRFs)  as well as
6:209:16318:16:16:71:534:310:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:care at home provided by home health agencies (HHAs) (MedPAC, 2015b). In 2013, Medicare paid
6:210:16410:16:17:71:537:294:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:$60 billion to PAC providers, approximately 16% of the $368 billion paid that year for Traditional
6:211:16508:17:16:71:537:277:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare (TM) claims (MedPAC, 2015a). This is about 20% larger than spending in Traditional
6:212:16599:16:16:71:343:261:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare on the much-studied Medicare Part D program.
6:213:16652:16:16:88:534:245:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In recent years, the geographic variation and growth rate of spending onPAC have raised
6:214:16739:16:17:72:532:229:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:concerns about the e¢ciency of the sector. From 2001 to 2013, Medicare spending on PAC grew
6:215:16830:17:16:72:536:212:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at an annual rate of 6.1%, 2 percentage points higher than the rate of total spending growth for
6:216:16926:16:16:72:535:196:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:TM (The Boards of Trustees for Medicare, 2002 and 2014; MedPAC, 2015a). A recent Institute
6:217:17016:16:17:72:534:180:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of Medicine report found that, despite accounting for only 16% of spending, PAC contributed to
6:218:17110:17:16:72:534:163:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a striking 73% of the unexplained geographic variation in spending, suggesting that there may be
6:219:17206:16:16:72:366:147:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:substantial ine¢ciencies in the sector (Newhouse et al., 2013).
6:220:17269:16:16:88:536:131:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:It is useful to think about patients generally owing downstream through the healthcare sys-
6:221:17363:16:17:71:536:115:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tem. Upon experiencing an acute health event, they enter a regular Acute Care Hospital (ACH);
6:222:17456:17:16:71:536:98:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from there they may enter a PAC facility to recover, and eventuallygo home once they are suf-
6:223:17549:16:0:71:533:82:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ciently healthy and can function independently. Some ACH patientsskip the PAC stay and
7:224:17639:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:return home directly from the ACH, and some patients occasionally relapseand move upstream
7:225:17731:17:16:71:246:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from a PAC facility back to an ACH.
7:226:17766:16:16:88:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The top panel of Figure 2 gives a sense of transitions among ACHs, PAC facilities (LTCHs,
7:227:17855:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:SNFs, and IRFs), home (including HHAs), and death (including hospice).(Throughout the rest
7:228:17945:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the paper, we use the term PAC facilities to refer to LTCHs, SNFs, andIRFs, because these
7:229:18037:17:16:71:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:are facilities that provide in-house care, in contrast to HHAs, whichprovide care at the patients
7:230:18135:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:home.) In our data, described below, 26% of patients who are discharged from an ACH receive
7:231:18226:16:17:71:532:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:follow-up care from a PAC facility.5From these PAC facilities, 60% of patients continue to ow
7:232:18320:17:16:71:533:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:home, where they may still receive treatment from an HHA, while 33% are discharged back to an
7:233:18413:16:16:71:410:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ACH. The remaining 7% of discharges are to a hospice or due to death.
7:234:18482:16:16:88:537:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Just like the natural ow of patients into and out of the PAC system, there is also a general
7:235:18575:16:17:71:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ordering of care within it. LTCHs provide the most intensive care,SNFs and IRFs provide less
7:236:18667:17:16:71:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:intensive care, and HHAs provide the least intensive bundle of medicalservices. Severity of Illness
7:237:18766:16:16:71:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(SOI) categories are a commonly used measure of intensity of care, and are constructed using the
7:238:18862:16:16:71:534:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients age, diagnoses, procedures, and comorbidities. The share of patients in the highest severity
7:239:18964:16:17:71:531:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of illness category declines from 43% at LTCHs, to approximately 12% at SNFs andIRFs, to 4%
7:240:19055:17:16:71:481:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at HHAs (AHA, 2010). Medicare payments per day follow the same declining pattern.
7:241:19136:16:16:88:537:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our point of entry into the PAC landscape is through admission to an LTCH. The bottom panel
7:242:19227:16:16:71:534:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of Figure 2 looks at patient ows from LTCHs. About 11% of LTCH patients are discharged back
7:243:19319:16:17:71:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to an ACH, 38% are discharged to another PAC facility (SNF or IRF), and 33% are discharged
7:244:19409:17:16:71:535:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to their homes, where they may continue to receive care from an HHA. The remaining 18% are
7:245:19499:16:16:71:537:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharged to a hospice (4%) or die within the LTCH (14%). In contrast,once in a SNF or IRF,
7:246:19591:16:16:71:535:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients almost never get discharged to an LTCH, die much less frequently (5%), and much more
7:247:19684:16:17:71:229:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:often (60%) return directly home.
7:248:19717:17:16:88:536:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Despite the interlocking nature of the PAC system, the way that Medicare reimburses post-
7:249:19806:16:16:72:534:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:acute care varies substantially by the setting. Historically, all providers were paid according to an
7:250:19907:16:16:72:534:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:administrative estimate of their costs. Since the early 2000s, however,many PAC stays are paid
7:251:20001:16:17:72:537:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:under a prospective payment system (PPS), yet the unit of paymentvaries across sites. Loosely,
7:252:20095:17:16:72:536:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:HHAs are paid per 60-day episode-of-care, SNFs are paid a xed rate per day of stay, while IRFs
7:253:20190:16:16:72:535:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and LTCHs are in principle paid a xed amount per admission (like ACHs).6We provide more
7:254:20278:16:16:71:259:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:details on LTCH payments in Section 3.
7:255:20316:16:17:88:534:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The fact that each type of facility is paid under a di¤erent system has raised concerns. From a
7:256:20411:17:18:71:536:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:public health perspective, there is concern that the separate payment systems do not give providers
7:257:20510:18:17:84:535:169:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:5In analysis that includes HHAs in the calculation, the shareof ACH patients who are discharged to PAC rises to
7:258:20621:17:10:72:165:152:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:42% (MedPAC, 2015b).
7:259:20641:10:17:84:536:142:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:6These di¤erent payment systems also have di¤erential implications for beneciaries cost-sharing requirements
7:260:20751:17:13:72:534:125:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:across types of PAC settings. Beneciaries generally are not required to make any cost sharing for HHA services. IRF
7:261:20867:13:13:72:535:112:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:and LTCH stays are tied to the beneciaries inpatient deductible, so when patients arrive from an ACH there would
7:262:20981:13:13:72:536:99:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:typically be no requirement for additional cost sharing. SNF stays are associated with separate SNF coinsurance for
7:263:21096:13:0:72:175:86:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:stays longer than 20 days.
8:264:21123:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:enough incentive to coordinate care across di¤erent facilities. Froma budgetary perspective, there
8:265:21221:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is concern that providers may shu­e patients across facilities with the aim of increasing Medicare
8:266:21319:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments.  These concerns have spurred various proposals for payment reform, including a re-
8:267:21411:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cent bill which proposes providing a bundled payment to a singlePAC coordinator, and letting
8:268:21505:16:17:71:533:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:this coordinator internalize the costs and benets associated with the sequence of admissions and
8:269:21602:17:32:71:433:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges for the entire episode of care (H.R.1458  BACPAC Act of 2015).
8:270:21676:32:23:71:242:594:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:2.2  Long-term care hospitals
8:271:21705:23:16:71:534:571:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our primary focus is on patients whose point of entry into the PAC system is a long-term care
8:272:21798:16:17:71:535:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hospital (LTCH). The demarcation LTCH describes how the provider gets paid by Medicare. It
8:273:21890:17:16:71:535:538:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is a regulatory concept, rather than a medical one. For a hospital to get paid as an LTCH, it must
8:274:21988:16:16:71:535:522:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have an average inpatient length of stay of 25 days or more. Naturally, thereare many ways to
8:275:22081:16:16:71:534:506:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:meet this requirement, so from a medical standpoint the question of what exactly is an LTCH often
8:276:22178:16:17:71:264:490:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:results in di¤erentiated or fuzzy answers.
8:277:22220:17:16:88:534:473:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The LTCH category of hospitals was created to solve a potential problem created by the 1982
8:278:22311:16:16:71:536:457:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Tax Equity and Fiscal Responsibility Act (TEFRA), which established the prospective payment
8:279:22402:16:16:71:533:441:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:system (PPS) for acute care hospitals. Under the new PPS, hospitals were paid per discharge, and
8:280:22498:16:17:71:535:425:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:not based on their costs, as a way to create incentives for hospitals to be e¢cient in their treatment
8:281:22600:17:16:71:536:408:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:decisions.  Regulators who were designing the PPS realized that therewas a small number of
8:282:22690:16:16:71:536:392:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hospitals that had long average length-of-stays (LOS) and would not be nancially viable under
8:283:22784:16:16:71:534:376:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the xed-price PPS. LTCHs were thus created as a carve-out from PPS for hospitals that had an
8:284:22877:16:17:71:534:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:average LOS of at least 25 days.  At that point in time, there were 40 hospitals that qualied
8:285:22971:17:16:71:534:343:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as LTCHs  mainly former tuberculosis and chronic disease hospitals in the Boston, New York
8:286:23062:16:16:71:536:327:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:City, and Philadelphia metropolitan areas. LTCH payments were based on costs measured in 1982,
8:287:23156:16:16:71:537:311:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:roughly in the spirit of the pre-1982 payment system, and adjusted for ination in subsequent years.
8:288:23256:16:17:71:402:295:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:See Liu et al. (2001) for more on the background of the LTCH sector.
8:289:23324:17:16:88:534:278:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Over the last 30 years, and perhaps because of the LTCH exemption from PPS, there was rapid
8:290:23415:16:16:72:536:262:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:growth in the LTCH sector. Because new entrants did not have cost data for 1982, payments for
8:291:23508:16:16:72:532:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:new entrants were determined by costs in their initial years of operation. This encouraged new
8:292:23602:16:17:72:536:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:entrants to be ine¢cient when they rst opened and earn prots by increasing their e¢ciency over
8:293:23698:17:16:72:535:213:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time (Liu et al., 2001). From the initial 40 hospitals rst designated as LTCHs in1982, there are
8:294:23795:16:16:72:275:197:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:now over 400 such hospitals in the country.
8:295:23838:16:17:88:537:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Geographic penetration of LTCHs is extremely varied. This presumably reects their historical
8:296:23931:17:16:72:533:164:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:roots as tuberculosis and chronic disease hospitals in the northeast,as well as certicate of need
8:297:24029:16:16:72:535:148:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(CON) laws that restrict entry. There are only a few LTCHs in the west of the country, and three
8:298:24125:16:16:72:534:132:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:states (Massachusetts, Texas, and Louisiana) account for a third of all LTCHs.In places where
8:299:24218:16:17:72:537:116:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:there are LTCHs, these hospitals are an important part of Medicares PAC landscape. For instance,
8:300:24315:17:16:72:530:99:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in hospital service areas (HSAs) with at least one LTCH, we calculate that LTCHs account for 13%
8:301:24411:16:0:72:536:83:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of Medicare PAC facility days and 28% of Medicare PAC facility spending;nationwide, payments
9:302:24504:0:17:71:374:708:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:to LTCHs account for 12% of Medicare PAC facility spending.7
9:303:24564:17:16:88:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs are much more likely to be for-prot than other medical providers.According to 2008
9:304:24653:16:16:71:535:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:data from the American Hospital Association (AHA), 72% of LTCHs are for-prot (versus 17% for
9:305:24746:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ACHs), 22% are non-prot, and 6% are government run. The LTCH market is dominated by two
9:306:24834:16:17:71:537:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for-prot companies, Kindred Health Systems and Select Medical, which run about 40% of LTCHs,
9:307:24927:17:16:71:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:according to the AHA data. Company reports indicate that LTCHs are highly protable. For their
9:308:25021:16:16:71:537:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:business segments that include LTCHs, Kindreds prots have hovered between 22% and 29% of
9:309:25111:16:17:71:424:594:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:revenue and Selects prots have ranged between 16% to 22% of revenue.8
9:310:25182:17:16:88:535:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Approximately half of LTCHs are known as Hospitals-within-Hospitals (HwHs), meaning that
9:311:25270:16:16:71:534:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:they are physically located within the building or campus of an ACH buthave a separate governing
9:312:25366:16:16:71:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:body and medical sta¤. Regardless of an LTCHs location (co-located or freestanding), they tend to
9:313:25464:16:17:71:533:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have strong relationships with a single ACH.9Because of concerns over close relationships between
9:314:25561:17:16:71:534:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs and their partner ACHs, in 2005 CMS established a policy known as the 25-percent rule
9:315:25654:16:16:71:537:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that creates disincentives for admitting more than 25% of patients from asingle facility; however,
9:316:25752:16:32:71:346:480:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:Congress has delayed the full implementation of the law.10
9:317:25810:32:23:71:124:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:2.3  Data
9:318:25819:23:17:71:535:425:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our main analysis focuses on patients who are admitted to an LTCH and follows them throughout
9:319:25912:17:16:71:535:408:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:their entire healthcare episode. Our primary data source is the Medicare Provider and Analysis
9:320:26006:16:16:71:536:392:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Review (MedPAR) data, spanning the years 2000-2012. The dataset contains claim-level informa-
9:321:26099:16:16:71:533:376:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tion on discharges from ACHs, LTCHs, SNFs, and IRFs. Each record is a unique stay for which
9:322:26190:16:17:71:535:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a claim was submitted, and the data contain information on procedures, admission and discharge
9:323:26284:17:16:71:535:343:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:dates, admission sources and discharge destinations, hospital charges,and Medicare payments. The
9:324:26380:16:16:71:535:327:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:MedPAR data also provide us with basic demographic information such as the age, sex, and race
9:325:26473:16:16:71:417:311:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the beneciary, and information about the patients diagnoses (DRG).
9:326:26544:16:17:88:536:295:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We supplement this primary source with several ancillary data sources. First, we use Medicares
9:327:26639:17:16:71:536:278:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:beneciary summary le on cost and use it to approximate post-LTCH discharge payments for
9:328:26728:16:18:71:535:262:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hospice and for HHAs, as well as post-LTCH discharge hospice days; Appendix A provides more
9:329:26819:18:17:84:533:244:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:7Authors calculations using 2007-2012 data described below. PAC facilities include LTCH, SNF and IRF. PAC
9:330:26925:17:13:72:537:227:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:facilities represent about 70% of total PAC spending, with the other 30% due to PAC provided by HHAs (MedPAC,
9:331:27034:13:10:72:99:214:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:2015a).
9:332:27041:10:17:84:535:204:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:8Prots are dened as EBITA (earnings before interest, taxes, and amortization). Kindreds prots are based on
9:333:27151:17:14:72:535:187:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:2009 to 2015 company reports. Prior to 2009, Kindred did not separate out their reporting of LTCH prots from the
9:334:27264:14:10:72:442:173:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:much larger SNF category. Selects prots are based on company reports from 2004 to 2015.
9:335:27353:10:17:84:534:163:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:9MedPAC (2004) found that HwHs receive 61% of their cases fromtheir most frequent referring hospital and
9:336:27457:17:10:72:384:146:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:freestanding hospitals receive 42% from their most frequent referring hospital.
9:337:27536:10:17:80:535:136:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:10There is also a regulation known as the 5-percent rule thataddresses the incentive for HwH to ping-pong
9:338:27645:17:13:72:533:119:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:patients between the ACH and LTCH. In particular, if more than 5% of patients who are discharged from an LTCH
9:339:27754:13:13:72:535:106:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:to an ACH are readmitted to the LTCH, the LTCH will be compensated as if the patient had a single LTCH stay
9:340:27861:13:0:72:144:93:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:(42 CFR 412.532).
10:341:27879:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:details.11Second, we use Medicares beneciary les to determine whether the beneciary is dually
10:342:27976:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:eligible for Medicare and Medicaid and the date of death (if any) through 2012. Third, we use the
10:343:28073:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare chronic conditions le to measure whether the individual hasany of 27 chronic conditions
10:344:28170:16:16:71:537:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the calendar year prior to the LTCH stay. Finally, we use data from the American Hospital
10:345:28262:16:17:71:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Association (AHA) survey over the same period to determine whether anLTCH is for-prot, non-
10:346:28354:17:16:71:413:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:prot, or government owned, and whether it is co-located with an ACH.
10:347:28423:16:16:88:536:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our analysis focuses on the current Medicare payment schedule for LTCHs, known as LTCH-
10:348:28510:16:17:71:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS. We analyze the time periods before and after full implementationof LTCH-PPS, which was
10:349:28601:17:16:71:535:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:phased in over a ve year period starting on October 1, 2002. We dene thepre-PPS periodas
10:350:28691:16:16:71:535:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges that occurred from January 1, 2000 to September 30, 2002. For this period, we measure
10:351:28787:16:16:71:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-discharge payments, days and mortality through March 31, 2003, which is six months after
10:352:28880:16:17:71:535:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the last LTCH discharge. We exclude the October 2002 to September 2007 phase-in period because
10:353:28974:17:16:71:537:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:provider behavior during this period potentially reects the combination of changing nancial
10:354:29067:16:16:71:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:incentives and learning about the new incentive structure, complicating the interpretation of the
10:355:29164:16:16:71:537:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:data. We dene thePPS periodas discharges that occurred from October 1, 2007 to July 31, 2012,
10:356:29258:16:17:71:535:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and analyze post-discharge payments, days and mortality through December 31, 2012, which is
10:357:29349:17:16:71:314:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:similarly six months after the last LTCH discharge.
10:358:29400:16:16:88:533:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Table 1 shows summary statistics on ACH, LTCH, and SNF/IRF admissions in the pre-PPS
10:359:29484:16:16:71:535:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and PPS periods.12Since an observation is an admission, some patients (16%) show up multiple
10:360:29576:16:17:71:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:times in the data. LTCH patients are, on average, slightly younger than ACHpatients and much
10:361:29668:17:16:71:534:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:younger than SNF/IRF patients. LTCH patients are also almost twice as likely to be black and
10:362:29760:16:16:71:537:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:about one-third more likely to be eligible for Medicaid, relative to ACH and SNF/IRF patients.
10:363:29854:16:16:71:534:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:These di¤erences are fairly stable over time. From a health perspective, patients in an LTCH look
10:364:29951:16:17:71:534:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:less healthy than those in an ACH or SNF/IRF. They have more chronic conditions prior to the stay
10:365:30048:17:16:71:534:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and higher mortality. For example, about 15% of LTCH patients die within 30days of admission
10:366:30140:16:16:71:535:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and 30% die within 90 days; these mortality rates are about 50% larger than mortality rates for
10:367:30235:16:16:71:407:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:SNF/IRF patients and about twice as large as those for ACH patients.
10:368:30303:16:17:88:535:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In terms of the intensity of medical care, LTCH stays are closer to ACH stays than stays at
10:369:30394:17:16:71:535:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a SNF/IRF. The majority of LTCH and ACH patients receive at least one medical procedure
10:370:30481:16:16:71:533:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:versus about 5% of patients who visit an SNF/IRF. The most common LTCH procedures (blood
10:371:30569:16:16:71:534:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:transfusion and ventilation) are also more similar to those that occur at an ACH, relative to
10:372:30662:16:17:71:534:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:occupational and physical therapies, which are the most common procedures in SNF/IRF. Length
10:373:30754:17:19:71:535:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of stay at an LTCH, however, is (by design) much more similar to that of a SNF/IRF. The average
10:374:30849:19:17:80:536:168:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:11In the MedPAR data, we can observe all discharge destinations, but we can only observe post-discharge payments
10:375:30961:17:13:71:533:151:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:and days for ACH and PAC facilities (SNF, IRF, LTCH) and not for home health visits or hospice. Appendix A
10:376:31067:13:13:71:537:138:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:shows how we estimate these using the summary le on cost and use which includes spending for all components of
10:377:31178:13:13:71:536:125:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:care. In practice, HHA and hospice payments are quite small as a share of the total. For example, we estimate that
10:378:31292:13:13:71:537:112:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:of individuals with an LTCH discharge, LTCH and SNF/IRF payments constitute over 90% of total PAC payments,
10:379:31399:13:10:71:238:99:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:with home health accounting for only 8%.
10:380:31439:10:17:80:536:89:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:12We group SNF and IRF admissions together for convenience, asboth represent post-acute care that is less
10:381:31545:17:0:71:486:72:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:intense than an LTCH and because IRFs only account for a small (6.4%) fraction of these admissions.
11:382:31646:0:17:71:446:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stay at an ACH is 5 days, while it is just over 25 days in LTCH and SNF/IRF.
11:383:31722:17:16:88:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The bottom rows of Table 1 show statistics on Medicare and out-of-pocket payments. Medicare
11:384:31813:16:16:72:533:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments in the PPS period average $2,079 per day at an ACH, $1,392 per day at an LTCH, and
11:385:31904:16:16:72:536:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:$507 per day at a SNF/IRF. However, because LTCH stays are much longer than ACH stays, per-
11:386:31995:16:17:72:534:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:admission Medicare payments at LTCHs average over $35,000, which is three times greater than
11:387:32087:17:16:72:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:per-admission ACH and SNF/IRF payments. Out-of-pocket payments at ACHs and LTCHs arise
11:388:32173:16:16:72:534:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from Medicares Part A deductible ($1,156 in 2012) and from co-insurance payments that apply
11:389:32265:16:17:72:537:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:when the patient has more than 60 hospital days in the benet period ($289per day in 2012).
11:390:32356:17:16:72:536:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Because patients have no out-of-pocket exposure between the deductible and their 60th hospital
11:391:32450:16:16:72:536:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:day, out-of-pocket payments are a modest 7.7% of Medicare payments at ACHs and 5.4% at LTCHs
11:392:32542:16:16:72:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the PPS period. SNFs, on the other hand, have a separate co-insurance schedule with payments
11:393:32637:16:17:72:535:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of $144.50 per day in 2012 for stays in excess of 20 days, and have a much higher out-of-pocket
11:394:32732:17:16:72:97:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:share.
11:395:32738:16:16:88:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our analysis encompasses not only the experience of the patient in theLTCH (i.e., length of stay
11:396:32834:16:16:72:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and payments) but also their post-discharge experience. Table 2 provides some summary statistics
11:397:32930:16:17:72:536:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on post-discharge experience. We dene a post-discharge episode of care as the spell of continuous
11:398:33028:17:16:72:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:days with a Medicare payment to an ACH, SNF/IRF or LTCH; the episode ends if there are two
11:399:33118:16:16:72:536:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:days or more without any Medicare payments being made to any of these institutions. For each post-
11:400:33216:16:16:72:537:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge episode we report 30-day mortality, 90-day mortality, post-discharge Medicare payments,
11:401:33313:16:17:72:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and post-discharge facility days (i.e. days in an ACH, SNF/IRF, LTCH or hospice). Focusing on
11:402:33406:17:16:72:535:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the PPS period, about one-quarter of LTCH patients die in the 90-days postdischarge. Average
11:403:33498:16:16:72:535:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:length of stay in the post-discharge episode of care is 26 days, whichis similar to the average time
11:404:33598:16:16:72:537:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the LTCH (see Table 1). Average post-discharge Medicare payments is $22,975, about 60% of
11:405:33690:16:17:72:295:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare payments to the LTCH (see Table 1).
11:406:33734:17:16:88:535:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In some of our analyses below, we will nd it useful to classify live discharges from the LTCH as
11:407:33831:16:16:72:535:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:either upstream or downstream based on their discharge destination. Upstream discharges
11:408:33924:16:16:72:532:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:represent patients in worse health than downstream destinations. Specically, we group LTCH
11:409:34017:16:17:72:536:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges to hospice or ACH as upstream and discharges to SNF/IRF, or home (with or with-
11:410:34109:17:16:72:537:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:out home healthcare), or to other as downstream.13Table 2 shows that most (about 85%) of
11:411:34199:16:16:71:536:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH discharges are downstream, and that patients initially discharged downstream have substan-
11:412:34294:16:16:71:530:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tially lower post-discharge mortality (e.g. 18% 90-day mortality compared to60% for upstream
11:413:34386:16:19:71:268:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges), length of stay, and payments.
11:414:34428:19:17:80:534:185:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:13Appendix Table A1 shows more granularity on the discharge destinations within upstream and downstream.  In
11:415:34536:17:13:71:538:168:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:the PPS period 97% of patients discharged upstream are sent to ACH (vs hospice); of patients discharged downstream,
11:416:34651:13:14:71:537:155:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:about half are sent to SNF/IRF (which we group together because over 90% of discharges to SNF or IRF are to SNF)
11:417:34763:14:0:71:320:141:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:and another 40% are discharged to home or home health care.
12:418:34823:0:28:71:451:708:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:3  LTCH payments, discharge patterns, and outcomes
12:419:34873:28:16:71:536:680:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In this section we present descriptive analysis on LTCHs responseto nancial incentives. We start
12:420:34971:16:16:71:534:664:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by describing the LTCH budget set, including the large jump in payments that is our primary
12:421:35062:16:16:71:534:648:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:source of identication. We then show evidence on how discharge patterns and mortality rates vary
12:422:35159:16:17:71:535:632:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with the budget set. This descriptive evidence motivates severalof our key modeling choices in the
12:423:35257:17:32:71:463:615:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:dynamic model of LTCH discharge behavior, which we present in the next section.
12:424:35336:32:23:71:193:583:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:3.1  LTCH payments
12:425:35354:23:16:71:536:560:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We provide a basic overview here of how LTCH payments vary with the patients length of stay,
12:426:35447:16:16:71:535:544:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an object we refer to as the LTCH budget set or payment schedule. Appendix B provides more
12:427:35537:16:17:71:479:528:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:details. Figure 1 summarizes the payment schedules in the pre-PPS and PPS periods.
12:428:35619:17:16:88:535:511:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Prior to October 1, 2002, LTCHs were paid their (estimated) daily cost, generating a linear
12:429:35710:16:16:71:536:495:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:relationship between the length of the hospital stay and payments.  Asdescribed earlier, this
12:430:35803:16:16:71:535:479:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cost plus reimbursement of LTCHs was seen as potentially encouraging ine¢cient entry into the
12:431:35898:16:17:71:534:463:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH market. Because of this and other concerns, the 1997 Balanced Budget Act (BBA) and
12:432:35985:17:16:71:535:446:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1999 Balanced Budget Renement Act (BBRA) implemented a PPS for LTCHs. LTCH-PPS was
12:433:36068:16:16:71:536:430:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:phased in over a 5-year period starting on October 1, 2002 and was fully implemented by October
12:434:36163:16:16:71:536:414:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1, 2007. At a broad level, LTCH-PPS is designed to operate like the PPS for acute care hospitals
12:435:36259:16:17:71:536:398:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(IP-PPS), under which hospitals are paid a lump-sum that is based on the patients diagnosis
12:436:36351:17:16:71:480:381:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(diagnosis-related group, or DRG) and does not vary with the patients length of stay.
12:437:36437:16:16:88:533:365:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Much like LTCHs were originally created to address a potential problem with the introduction
12:438:36529:16:16:71:535:349:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of PPS for ACHs, the features of the LTCH-PPS payment schedule can similarly be thought of as
12:439:36622:16:17:71:533:333:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:attempting to address a potential problem arising from the introduction of PPS for LTCHs. In
12:440:36714:17:16:71:535:316:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:particular, in designing LTCH-PPS, o¢cials were concerned that LTCHs might discharge patients
12:441:36807:16:16:71:535:300:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:after a small number of days but still receive large lump-sum paymentsintended for longer hospitals
12:442:36906:16:17:71:535:284:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stays. To address this concern, they created a short stay outlier (SSO) threshold. If stays were
12:443:37002:17:16:71:536:267:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shorter than the SSO threshold, payments would be based on the pre-PPScost-based reimbursement
12:444:37096:16:16:71:535:251:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule and LTCHs would not receive a large lump sum. However, while reducing the incentive to
12:445:37191:16:16:71:536:235:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cycle patients in and out of the LTCH, the SSO system creates potentially problematic incentives
12:446:37287:16:17:71:530:219:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at the SSO threshold. At the day when payments switch from per-day reimbursement to lump-sum
12:447:37379:17:16:71:535:202:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:prospective payment amount, Medicare payments for keeping a patient anadditional day jump
12:448:37470:16:16:71:158:186:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by a large amount.
12:449:37488:16:16:88:536:170:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 1 graphs the average payment schedules in the pre-PPS and PPSperiods, pooling across
12:450:37579:16:17:71:533:154:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH facilities and DRGs. The y-axis shows cumulative Medicare payments, ination-adjusted
12:451:37669:17:16:71:535:137:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to 2012 dollars. The x-axis shows the length of the stay relative to the SSO threshold, which we
12:452:37765:16:16:71:536:121:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:normalize to be day 0. The SSO threshold is dened as ve-sixths the geometric mean length of
12:453:37858:16:16:71:535:105:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stay for that DRG in the previous year and therefore varies by DRG (and also, to a much lesser
12:454:37952:16:0:71:531:89:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:extent, by year). The average threshold is at 22.5 days; the modal threshold (accounting for 22.4%
13:455:38052:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of PPS stays) is 20 days and 99% of the sample has a SSO threshold between 16 and39 days.14As
13:456:38145:17:16:72:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a result, in this and subsequent gures, we present results relative to the SSO threshold so that we
13:457:38245:16:16:72:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:can pool analyses across DRGs.15Because the SSO threshold is undened in the pre-PPS period,
13:458:38337:16:16:71:416:659:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:we assign pre-PPS stays the threshold for their DRG from PPS period.16
13:459:38407:16:17:88:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Under the pre-PPS system, average payments scale linearly with the length of stay at a rate of
13:460:38501:17:16:72:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:$1,071 per day. Under the PPS system, payments increase linearly by $1,386 per day to the left of
13:461:38598:16:16:72:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the SSO threshold, jump by $13,656 at the SSO threshold, and remain constant thereafter. The
13:462:38692:16:17:72:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increase in payments at the jump is large: it is equal to 55% of the cumulative payment amount
13:463:38786:17:16:72:534:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on the day prior to the threshold, or equivalent to about 10 days of payments at the pre-threshold
13:464:38884:16:16:72:118:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:daily rate.
13:465:38895:16:16:88:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This sharp jump in payments was presumably not the intention of the policymakers who de-
13:466:38983:16:17:72:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:signed the LTCH-PPS, but it arises naturally from the interaction oftwo sensible policies. As is
13:467:39079:17:16:72:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:standard in xed price contracts, the LTCH-PPS payments were likely set to approximate average
13:468:39173:16:16:72:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:costs per stay. As noted, payments on a cost-plus basis up to the SSO threshold were introduced to
13:469:39271:16:16:72:536:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:avoid paying LTCHs large lump sum amounts for relatively short stays.While reducing the incen-
13:470:39365:16:17:72:534:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tive to discharge patients from an ACH to an LTCH, the transition unavoidably creates potentially
13:471:39462:17:16:72:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:problematic incentives. Particularly concerning is where the threshold was set: we estimate that
13:472:39558:16:16:72:534:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:under the pre-PPS payment scheme, 44% of stays would have been below the subsequent short stay
13:473:39652:16:16:72:536:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:outlier threshold, which is a large fraction for a policy that is at leastostensibly designed to target
13:474:39755:16:17:72:147:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:outlier events.
13:475:39772:17:16:88:536:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In Section 4 we explore the impact of alternative, counterfactual payment schedules. To elim-
13:476:39865:16:16:72:534:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:inate the jump in payments, our counterfactuals alter the payment prior to the SSO threshold
13:477:39957:16:16:72:535:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(so that it does not approximate per-day costs), alter the xed PPS amount (so that it does not
13:478:40052:16:32:72:434:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:approximate average costs), or alter both segments of the payment schedule.
13:479:40127:32:24:72:208:302:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:3.2  Discharge patterns
13:480:40150:24:16:72:535:278:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We present a number of descriptive results on discharge patterns from the LTCH around the
13:481:40239:16:16:72:535:262:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold. The results can be summarized as follows. First, there is a large spike in discharges
13:482:40335:16:16:72:536:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at precisely the day of the jump in payments, indicating a strong response to nancial incentives.
13:483:40433:16:17:72:535:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Second, the marginal patients discharged at the threshold are in relatively better health: they are
13:484:40532:17:16:72:535:213:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:disproportionately discharged downstream and they have lower post-discharge mortality rates
13:485:40625:16:16:72:536:197:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:than patients discharged at other times. Third, among patients discharged downstream, the mar-
13:486:40719:16:19:72:536:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ginal patients discharged at the threshold are relatively sicker, with higher post-discharge payments
13:487:40820:19:13:80:342:162:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:14Across all DRGs, the SSO threshold ranges from 14 to 56 days.
13:488:40883:13:17:80:536:149:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:15We start the x-axis range at -15 days because nearly all SSO thresholds occur after 16 days. If we extended the
13:489:40996:17:14:71:535:132:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:x-axis range to -16, for example, there would be a change in the composition of DRGs between days -16 and -15
13:490:41105:14:13:71:533:118:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:due to the entry of new DRGs into the sample. We end the x-axis range at 45 days because there are relatively few
13:491:41218:13:10:71:434:105:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:(2.1%) patients who are kept at the LTCH more than 45 days beyond the SSO threshold.
13:492:41302:10:17:80:535:95:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:16Since the thresholds occasionally vary over time, we use theSSO threshold from 2007, which is the rst year in
13:493:41414:17:0:72:134:78:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:the PPS period.
14:494:41431:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:than pre-threshold dischargees. Below we discuss how these patternscan be rationalized with a
14:495:41524:17:16:71:224:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:simple model of LTCH behavior.
14:496:41554:16:16:88:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 3 shows the aggregate pattern of discharges by length of stay in the pre-PPS and PPS
14:497:41645:16:16:71:536:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:periods. A discharge occurs when the patient is transferred to another facility, sent home, or dies at
14:498:41747:16:17:71:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the LTCH. The y-axis shows discharges as a share of the total number of stays at the LTCH. The x-
14:499:41844:17:16:71:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:axis plots the length of stay relative to the DRG-specic SSO threshold, dened in the same manner
14:500:41942:16:16:71:534:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as in Figure 1. In the PPS period, there is a sharp increase in discharges at the SSO threshold, with
14:501:42043:16:17:71:534:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the share of discharges increasing from about 2% to 9% per day. Discharge rates remain elevated
14:502:42138:17:16:71:534:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:over the subsequent 7 to 10 days before reverting to baseline. Inthe pre-PPS period, there is no
14:503:42234:16:16:71:535:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evidence of any bunching at the SSO threshold. This discharge pattern is consistent with a strong
14:504:42331:16:16:71:237:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:response to the nancial incentives.
14:505:42367:16:17:88:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Relative to the pre-PPS constant, per-day payment schedule, the increase in discharges under
14:506:42459:17:16:72:537:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS at the threshold could be drawn either from the left or from theright of the distribution.
14:507:42557:16:16:72:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In other words, the excess discharges at the threshold could reect patients who under the pre-PPS
14:508:42655:16:16:72:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule would have been discharged before the threshold but are retained in order to get the
14:509:42750:16:17:72:533:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:lump sum payout at the threshold, or patients who would have been discharged after the threshold
14:510:42846:17:16:72:533:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but are now discharged earlier since there is no longer a marginal nancialpayment associated with
14:511:42944:16:16:72:229:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:keeping them for additional days.
14:512:42977:16:16:88:535:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Because the share of discharges to the left of the threshold is lower in the PPS period relative to
14:513:43076:16:17:72:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the pre-PPS period, it is tempting to infer that the excess mass of discharges is primarily drawn
14:514:43174:17:16:72:535:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from the left of the distribution. However, we caution that di¤erences in the discharge rate might
14:515:43273:16:16:72:536:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:not only reect the change in nancial incentives but also changes in patient health and other secular
14:516:43375:16:16:72:534:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:trends between the pre-PPS and PPS periods. For example, a simple reweighting of the pre-PPS
14:517:43467:16:17:72:535:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:admissions to match the DRG composition of the PPS period eliminates approximately 30% of the
14:518:43560:17:16:72:534:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:di¤erence between the pre-PPS and PPS discharge rates prior to theSSO threshold (Appendix
14:519:43649:16:16:72:535:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure A2).  In Section 4, we show how we can use our model to compare discharge patterns
14:520:43738:16:16:72:536:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:under the observed PPS payment schedule to discharge patterns under alternative, counterfactual
14:521:43833:16:17:72:161:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment schedules.
14:522:43851:17:16:88:534:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 4 decomposes the discharge pattern by the location of discharge inorder of descending
14:523:43943:16:16:71:534:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patient health: downstream, upstream, and death. The gure shows increases at the threshold in
14:524:44037:16:16:71:535:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges both upstream and downstream, but the proportional increaseis substantially larger
14:525:44130:16:17:71:536:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on the downstream margin. Moreover, because the pre-threshold discharge rate is much higher
14:526:44221:17:16:71:535:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:downstream, the sharp change in discharge rate at the threshold (shown inFigure 3) is almost
14:527:44313:16:16:71:534:171:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:entirely driven by downstream discharges. We defer our discussion ofthe bottom panel on mortality
14:528:44410:16:17:71:183:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to the subsection below.
14:529:44434:17:16:88:534:138:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Figure A3 plots the 30-day post-discharge mortality rate, dened as death within
14:530:44523:16:16:71:535:122:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:30 days of a (live) discharge, by length of stay. The graph shows a sharp drop in post-discharge
14:531:44619:16:16:71:535:106:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality at the SSO threshold, again suggesting that the patients who are discharged at the
14:532:44711:16:17:71:537:90:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold are healthier than the patients who are discharged immediately beforehand. Of course,
14:533:44806:17:0:71:535:73:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the decline in mortality not only reects changes in the composition of patients discharged at the
15:534:44906:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold, but could in principle reect a treatment e¤ect of discharge on health. We address this
15:535:45004:17:16:71:160:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the next section.
15:536:45024:16:16:88:531:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, Figure 5 plots Medicare payments for the episode of care that occurs after the LTCH
15:537:45116:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge, by length of stay at the LTCH. We show these post-discharge payments separately
15:538:45206:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for patients discharged upstream and discharged downstream and view themas a proxy for
15:539:45296:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the patients health at the time of discharge.  For patients discharged downstream, there is a
15:540:45390:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:sharp increase in post-discharge payments at the threshold, with average post-discharge payments
15:541:45486:16:17:71:534:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increasing from approximately $10,000 to $20,000. There is a small change in the opposite direction
15:542:45585:17:16:71:535:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for patients initially discharged upstream. For longer lengths of stay, the gure becomes noisy due
15:543:45684:16:16:71:232:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to the small number of discharges.
15:544:45718:16:16:88:534:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 5 suggests a simple model of LTCH behavior, which motivates the model we present in
15:545:45808:16:17:72:535:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Section 4. Prior to the threshold, retaining patients is protable,and only the healthiest patients are
15:546:45911:17:16:72:537:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharged to SNF/IRF or their home and the sickest patients discharged to an ACH or a hospice.
15:547:46006:16:16:72:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:After the threshold, on the downstream margin, LTCHs work down the distribution and discharge
15:548:46101:16:16:72:531:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:less healthy patients, increasing post-discharge payments on average.Similarly, on the upstream
15:549:46196:16:17:72:534:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:margin, LTCHs work up the distribution, discharging patients who are in better health, and
15:550:46288:17:16:72:536:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:decreasing post-discharge payments on average. The marginal patient discharged downstream at
15:551:46380:16:16:72:535:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the threshold is therefore sicker than the average patient discharged prior to the threshold, while
15:552:46479:16:16:72:534:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the marginal patient discharged upstream is slightly healthier thanthe average patient discharged
15:553:46576:16:32:72:181:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:upstream in prior days.
15:554:46599:32:24:72:253:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:3.3  (Lack of) Mortality e¤ects
15:555:46630:24:16:72:536:343:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:A natural question raised by the discharge patterns is whether the distortions in the timing of
15:556:46725:16:16:72:534:327:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharges have an impact on patient health and in particular mortality. Since the 90-day mortality
15:557:46824:16:16:72:536:311:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rate of LTCH patients is approximately 30% (see Table 1), if these distortions are harmful to health,
15:558:46925:16:17:72:430:295:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:it seems plausible that we might be able to pick up an e¤ect with our data.
15:559:47000:17:16:95:535:278:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Empirical identication of mortality e¤ects from the distortion in patient location at the
15:560:47090:16:16:72:536:262:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold is challenging, however.  Health evolves according to a stochastic process, with sicker
15:561:47187:16:16:72:535:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients having a higher probability of death. Distortions to the location of care might impact
15:562:47282:16:17:72:536:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the level of someones health, generating an on-impact e¤ect on the probability of death analogous
15:563:47380:17:16:72:535:213:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to the on-impact e¤ect on discharges we detected.  However, distortionsto the location might
15:564:47472:16:16:72:534:197:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:also a¤ect the stochastic process for health, which would be associated with a longer-run change in
15:565:47571:16:17:72:535:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality rate, but might not have an immediate mortality e¤ect. We therefore attempt to examine
15:566:47667:17:16:72:534:164:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:not only whether there is an immediate impact on mortality at the threshold, but whether we can
15:567:47762:16:16:72:215:148:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:detect any longer-run changes.
15:568:47792:16:16:88:536:132:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We have already seen in the bottom panel of Figure 4 some suggestive evidence that mortal-
15:569:47882:16:17:71:532:116:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ity rates are declining over the course of the LTCH stay with little di¤erence around the SSO
15:570:47975:17:18:71:534:99:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold.17However, the interpretation of the bottom panel of Figure 4  which plots mortality
15:571:48070:18:0:80:536:81:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:17The downward trend results from natural selection: as thesickest patients die, the remaining patients become
16:572:48184:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rates for LTCH patients by length of stay  is complicated by selection concerns. Since LTCHs are
16:573:48281:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:di¤erentially discharging healthier patients at the SSO threshold,the composition of patients who
16:574:48378:16:16:71:533:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:remain at the LTCH is changing, making it tricky to disentangle any potentialtreatment e¤ects on
16:575:48474:16:16:71:412:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality from e¤ects due to changes in the selection of LTCH patients.
16:576:48545:16:17:88:534:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In Figure 6 we circumvent this issue by taking advantage of the fact thatour data allow us to
16:577:48638:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:track mortality outcomes for patients even after their LTCH discharge. Conceptually, our mortality
16:578:48736:16:16:71:537:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:analysis follows the logic of a reduced form regression, where the mortality hazard is the outcome,
16:579:48835:16:17:71:533:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge patterns are the endogenous variable, and the SSO threshold isthe instrument.  In
16:580:48926:17:16:71:536:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:particular, since we know there is a sharp jump in discharge patterns at the threshold (analogous
16:581:49023:16:16:71:535:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to a large rst stage), if there is a change in the level or slope of the mortalityhazard at the
16:582:49119:16:16:71:534:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold (that is, non-zero reduced form), we can infer that the distortion in discharge location
16:583:49217:16:17:71:200:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:has an impact on mortality.
16:584:49244:17:16:88:537:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The top panel of Figure 6 is thus similar to the bottom panel of Figure 4, butuses the full
16:585:49335:16:16:72:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:set of LTCH patients (unconditional on their location) rather than onlythose who have yet to be
16:586:49430:16:16:72:534:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharged. As before, natural selection leads mortality rates to decline over time, but we now can
16:587:49529:16:17:72:536:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:interpret more cleanly the mortality pattern around the SSO threshold. The plot shows no obvious
16:588:49625:17:16:72:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evidence of a change in the level of mortality hazard in the vicinity of the threshold during the
16:589:49722:16:16:72:534:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS period. In Appendix C, we examine this mortality pattern more formallyusing a regression
16:590:49814:16:16:72:535:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discontinuity design and similarly fail to reject the null of a smooth mortality hazard around the
16:591:49912:16:17:72:535:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:SSO threshold. These ndings are consistent with no mortality e¤ect but do not allow us to rule
16:592:50007:17:16:72:371:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:out a gradual e¤ect that would not appear sharply in the data.
16:593:50069:16:16:88:535:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:If distortions in the location of care a¤ected the stochastic process forhealth, we might not
16:594:50162:16:16:72:535:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:observe an immediate e¤ect, but would see a change in mortality over a longer time horizon. The
16:595:50257:16:17:72:534:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:bottom panel of Figure 6 attempts to look for a more gradual e¤ect by plotting a 30-day mortality
16:596:50354:17:16:72:536:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rate, by days since LTCH admission, where the 30-day mortality hazard measures the share of
16:597:50445:16:16:72:534:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients who are alive on a given day but die during the subsequent30 days. The plot once again
16:598:50540:16:16:72:534:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:shows no e¤ect around the threshold, suggesting that there are no gradual e¤ects of the distortion
16:599:50638:16:17:72:534:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in location on mortality. In Appendix C, we present a regression discontinuity analysis that more
16:600:50735:17:16:72:207:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:formally conrms this result.
16:601:50764:16:16:88:534:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 6 suggests little evidence of a quantitatively large e¤ect on mortality that is created by
16:602:50861:16:16:72:535:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the sharp discharge incentives at the SSO threshold. This (lack of) reduced form e¤ect should be
16:603:50957:16:17:71:532:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:judged in relation to the size of the rst stage e¤ect on the location of care. And as we will show
16:604:51056:17:16:72:534:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the analysis in the next section, the jump in payments at the threshold only shifts the location
16:605:51155:16:16:72:491:171:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of care for a relatively small number of days, so perhaps the null resultis not surprising.
16:606:51246:16:17:88:536:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, we provide some additional traction on potential mortality impacts by exploiting dif-
16:607:51340:17:16:72:535:138:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ferences in the response to incentives between for-prot and non-prot LTCHs. Figure 7 shows
16:608:51432:16:16:72:531:122:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the payment schedules (top panel), discharge shares (middle panel),and mortality rates (bottom
16:609:51526:16:23:72:535:106:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:panel), separately by for-prot status. While the payment schedules are almost the same across
16:610:51620:23:0:71:147:83:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:gradually healthier.
17:611:51642:0:17:71:536:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the two groups of hospitals, the behavioral response to the jump in payments is substantially larger
17:612:51742:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at for-prot LTCHs.  Prior to the threshold, for-prot hospitals have a slightly lower discharge
17:613:51838:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rate, but at the SSO threshold their discharge share rises by about twice as much as that of non-
17:614:51935:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:prot hospitals, suggesting that, perhaps not surprisingly, for-prot hospitals are relatively more
17:615:52034:16:17:71:227:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:responsive to nancial incentives.
17:616:52068:17:16:88:537:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Given that for-prots LTCHs exhibit much larger behavioral response tothe jump in payments,
17:617:52159:16:16:71:536:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:if the PPS payment schedule a¤ected mortality, we would expect a more pronounced mortality e¤ect
17:618:52255:16:17:71:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at for-prot hospitals. The bottom panel of Figure 7 provides no suggestion of any di¤erences in the
17:619:52355:17:16:71:535:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality pattern by for-prot status. Mortality hazards in the PPS period are remarkably similar
17:620:52452:16:16:71:536:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:across both groups in the immediate vicinity of the SSO threshold; this suggests no on-impact e¤ect
17:621:52551:16:16:71:260:545:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:of the location distortion on mortality.18
17:622:52593:16:17:88:532:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While these results provide no smoking gun evidence of patient harm, they do not allow
17:623:52681:17:16:71:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:us to comprehensively rule out a mortality e¤ect. We cannot, for instance, rule out an e¤ect for
17:624:52777:16:16:71:536:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:every type of patient or at each and every hospital, and we cannot rule outadverse health e¤ects
17:625:52873:16:16:71:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that would not manifest in higher mortality rates.  However, combined with the earlier results
17:626:52967:16:17:71:534:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which indicate that the patients who are most a¤ected by the SSO threshold are disproportionately
17:627:53064:17:16:71:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:healthy, the results suggest that the marginal patients a¤ected by the PPS payment schedule are
17:628:53159:16:16:71:537:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:able to receive similar care whether they are located in an LTCH or in their alternative setting,
17:629:53256:16:38:71:237:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which empirically is usually a SNF.
17:630:53291:38:27:71:442:377:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:4  Quantifying the importance of nancial incentives
17:631:53343:27:16:71:534:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The results in the last section provide descriptive evidence of the response of LTCHs to the sharp
17:632:53441:16:17:71:535:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:nancial incentives associated with the SSO threshold. One way to quantify the importance of the
17:633:53537:17:16:71:534:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:nancial incentives in determining LTCH discharge patterns is to assess how these patterns would
17:634:53633:16:16:71:535:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:change in response to counterfactual nancial contracts that exhibit weaker incentives. Doing so
17:635:53729:16:32:71:356:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:requires a dynamic model, which is the focus of this section.
