19:739:57899:32:24:72:376:532:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:4.2  Parameterization, estimation, and identication
19:740:57951:24:16:72:537:508:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Parameterization.We make several additional assumptions before we take the model to the data.
19:741:58044:16:16:72:539:492:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f6:First, we restrict the set of alternative discharge destinationsJto include only two options,J=
19:742:58139:16:16:72:534:476:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f3:fD; Ug. Motivated by the summary statistics described in Section 3, optionDcovers the collection
19:743:58235:16:17:72:535:460:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of downstream destinations (SNF, IRF, and home with or without HHA) that are appropriate
19:744:58323:17:16:72:533:443:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for LTCH patients who are of better health or require lower levels ofmedical monitoring.  In
19:745:58415:16:16:72:535:427:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:contrast, optionUcovers upstream discharge destinations (ACH and hospice) which wouldbe
19:746:58502:16:16:72:390:411:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:natural discharge destinations for patients who are of worse health.
19:747:58570:16:17:88:536:395:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Because, conditional on discharge, nancial incentives do not a¤ect theLTCHs discharge des-
19:748:58662:17:16:72:537:378:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tination, having a richer set of discharge options is unlikely to a¤ect our counterfactual predictions.
19:749:58765:16:16:72:537:362:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:By focusing on two options, we essentially restrict the LTCH to consider two types of marginal
19:750:58859:16:16:72:537:346:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH patients. One set of marginal patient are those who are healthier, and for whom the hospital
19:751:58956:16:17:72:537:330:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:must consider whether to keep them or discharge them to locationD. The second set of marginal
19:752:59049:17:16:72:532:313:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients are sicker, and for whom the hospital must consider whether to keep them in the LTCH
19:753:59142:16:16:72:220:297:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or transfer them to locationU.
19:754:59172:16:16:88:540:281:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The second assumption is about the health process. Given that mortality is monotone inh,
19:755:59260:16:17:72:535:265:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:it is convenient to normalize the health index by mortality risk. Wedo so by assuming thathis
19:756:59353:17:27:72:424:248:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:dened by its associated mortality hazard using the following relationship:
19:757:59428:27:27:264:536:221:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:m(h) = 1-\b(h),(6)
19:758:59446:27:16:72:536:194:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where\b(-)is the standard normal CDF. We note thathis an index and thus has no cardinal mean-
19:759:59539:16:17:72:534:178:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ing, and the above is simply a normalization, which endowshwith a cardinal measure. Equipped
19:760:59631:17:16:72:536:161:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with this normalization, we then make parametric assumptions about the initial (as of LTCH ad-
19:761:59725:16:16:72:535:145:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mission,t= 0) health distribution of newly admitted patients, and about how the health process
19:762:59819:16:16:72:534:129:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evolves over time within the LTCH. Specically, we assume thathi;0is drawn fromN(-0; -20)and
19:763:59911:16:28:71:295:113:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:thatF(-jhi;t-1)follows a simple AR(1) process:
19:764:59957:28:0:196:536:85:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:hi;t=-+-hi;t-1+"i;t;where"i;t-N(0; -2).(7)
20:765:60001:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In our baseline specication, we allow the health process to be di¤erent inside the LTCH in the
20:766:60096:17:16:71:533:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:pre-PPS and PPS periods to accommodate potential di¤erences in the LTCH patient mix, which
20:767:60186:16:16:71:537:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:may result from the growth of the LTCH sector, time trends in medical technology and practice,
20:768:60280:16:16:71:533:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or directly from the change in nancial incentives. Note that we do not need to model the health
20:769:60376:16:17:71:534:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:process outside the LTCH since any e¤ect of the post-discharge location on health would a¤ect the
20:770:60473:17:16:71:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge decisions through its (unidentied) e¤ect on the continuation values,Vj(h), which we do
20:771:60570:16:16:71:166:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:not model explicitly.
20:772:60591:16:27:88:534:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The third assumption is associated with the LTCHs costs,cl(h), which we assume are given by
20:773:60683:27:28:264:536:567:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:cl(h) =\rcreported.(8)
20:774:60705:28:16:71:535:539:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:That is, as described in Section 2 and reported in the bottom row of Table1, we observe the
20:775:60796:16:16:71:535:523:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cost associated with each hospitall, which enters the formula by which it is paid by CMS. We
20:776:60888:16:16:71:536:507:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:use the averages reported there (separately for the pre-PPS and PPS periods) as our estimates of
20:777:60984:16:17:71:536:491:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:creported.  We do not treat these reported costs as the true costs, but we use it to guide our
20:778:61080:17:16:72:534:474:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:model of costs in two ways. First, and importantly, we assume that the hospitals cost do not vary
20:779:61178:16:16:72:539:458:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with the patient healthh, which is consistent with CMSs treatment of costs and also seems natural
20:780:61276:16:17:72:535:442:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:given that LTCH patients are generally stable. Second, we assume that the reported costs are true
20:781:61373:17:16:72:536:425:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:up to a monotone transformation, which we assume to be linear.  This assumption means that
20:782:61463:16:16:72:536:409:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:if hospitallhas higher reported costs than hospitalk, we will also assume that this also reects
20:783:61559:16:16:72:533:393:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the ranking of their true underlying costs.  This seems (to us) reasonable, and could be driven
20:784:61654:16:17:72:536:377:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by a variety of factors, including geographic location. We expect\r-1, but do not impose this
20:785:61747:17:16:72:186:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:constraint in estimation.
20:786:61772:16:16:88:535:344:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The fourth assumption is to parameterizeVU(h)andVD(h).  We approximate each using a
20:787:61855:16:16:71:200:328:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:linear function inh, so that
20:788:61883:16:23:228:536:312:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:Vj(h) =-0j+-1jhforj=D; U.(9)
20:789:61911:23:16:71:535:289:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Recall from Section 3 that healthier patients (higherh), who are associated with lower mortality, are
20:790:62012:16:17:72:534:273:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharged toD, while sicker patients (lowerh), associated with higher mortality, are discharged to
20:791:62111:17:16:71:535:256:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:U. It is therefore natural to expect-1;D>0andv1;U<0. That is, all else equal, facilityDbecomes
20:792:62205:16:16:71:534:240:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a more attractive discharge destination as health gets better (his higher) and facilityUbecomes a
20:793:62302:16:16:72:537:224:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:more attractive discharge destination as patients health worsens (his lower). As explained below,
20:794:62400:16:17:71:471:208:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:one of the intercept termsv0;Dandv0;Uneeds to be normalized, so we setv0;U= 0.
20:795:62478:17:16:88:537:191:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Fifth, as we discussed in Section 2, LTCHs are part of an interlocking post-acute care system,
20:796:62572:16:16:71:537:175:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with changes in LTCH incentives potentially a¤ecting Medicare spending throughout the patients
20:797:62667:16:16:71:536:159:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:entire episode of care. In particular, Figure 5 shows sharp changes inboth upstream and down-
20:798:62760:16:17:71:537:143:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stream post-discharge payments at the SSO threshold, indicating a relationship between patients
20:799:62856:17:16:71:534:126:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:health at discharge and total Medicare spending. To account for this typeof spillover e¤ects in
20:800:62951:16:16:71:535:110:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:our counterfactuals, we model the relationship between health at discharge and post-discharge
20:801:63044:16:16:71:125:94:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments as
20:802:63055:16:0:214:535:78:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:Pj(h) = exp(-0;j+-1;jh)forj=D; U,(10)
21:803:63094:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:wherePj(h)represents the post-discharge payments for a patient initially discharged to location
21:804:63189:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:j=D; Uwith health statushat the time of discharge. We allow this relationship to vary by whether
21:805:63285:16:16:71:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the patient is initially discharged upstream or downstream and use anexponential specication so
21:806:63381:16:16:71:355:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that predicted post-discharge payments are strictly positive.
21:807:63442:16:17:88:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, as is typical in these types of models, we set (rather than estimate) the daily discount
21:808:63539:17:16:72:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:factor to-= 0:951=365.20Thus, overall we are left with 19 parameters to estimate: ve parame-
21:809:63632:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ters (-0; -0; -; -; -) that are associated with the health distribution and the way it evolves over
21:810:63731:16:17:71:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time in the pre-PPS period, ve corresponding parameters in the PPSperiod, the cost parameters
21:811:63825:17:16:71:537:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(\r), four parameters (-1U; -0D; -1D; --) associated with the relative value of patients at facili-
21:812:63924:16:16:71:534:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tiesUandD, and four parameters associated with post-discharge payments in the PPS period
21:813:64012:16:19:71:172:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(-0;D; -1;D; -0;U; -1;U).
21:814:64037:19:17:88:535:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Estimation.An important decision is how to treat heterogeneity across patients, observable
21:815:64127:17:16:71:536:509:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:health conditions, and LTCH hospitals. In our baseline specication,we abstract from such hetero-
21:816:64224:16:16:71:532:493:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:geneity and instead model the average discharge decision as it pertains to the average LTCH
21:817:64319:16:16:71:533:477:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patient and the average payment schedule. That is, we pool all payment schedules observed in
21:818:64413:16:17:71:534:461:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the data, separately for the pre-PPS and PPS periods, measure each dayin the schedule relative to
21:819:64510:17:16:71:536:444:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the DRG-specic SSO threshold in the PPS period (which is normalized to day zero), and construct
21:820:64606:16:16:71:535:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the average payment schedule for each day, as shown in Figure 1. We thenapply an analogous
21:821:64696:16:16:71:534:412:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:exercise to the discharge pattern, and construct the distribution ofdischarge patterns in a 61-day
21:822:64794:16:17:71:535:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:window around the SSO threshold (from day -15 to day 45), as shown in Figure 3 and Figure 4. We
21:823:64889:17:16:71:535:379:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:then estimate our model in an attempt to match these average patterns. The dramatic di¤erence
21:824:64982:16:16:71:537:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the payment schedules between the pre-PPS and PPS periods will assist in the identication of
21:825:65078:16:16:71:455:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:some of the parameters and it is an important component of our research design.
21:826:65157:16:17:88:536:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:An advantage of this approach of focusing on the average pattern rather than the heterogenous
21:827:65250:17:16:71:534:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:pattern is that it only requires us to solve the dynamic problem once (foreach period, pre-PPS and
21:828:65348:16:16:71:535:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS), which is computationally attractive. Given that our primary focus is on the aggregate e¤ect
21:829:65445:16:16:71:536:282:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of nancial incentives across the entire LTCH sector, abstracting from the heterogeneity across pa-
21:830:65544:16:17:71:535:266:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tients and hospitals is likely to be inconsequential. That said, motivated by the descriptive evidence
21:831:65646:17:16:71:534:249:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in how for-prot and non-prot LTCHs di¤erentially respond to nancial incentives, we separately
21:832:65742:16:16:71:535:233:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:conduct a counterfactual analysis to explore the heterogeneous e¤ectsof nancial incentives for
21:833:65837:16:16:71:535:217:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:these two types of hospitals, which reveals some intriguing patterns.Heterogeneity in the response
21:834:65935:16:17:71:416:201:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is also the focus of the related exercise reported by Eliason et al. (2016).
21:835:66011:17:16:88:534:184:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We estimate the model using simulated method of moments, by trying to match the daily
21:836:66096:16:16:71:535:168:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality and discharge patterns presented in Figure 4, as well as post-discharge payment moments
21:837:66193:16:16:71:536:152:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that are based on Figure 5. Specically, we use two sets of moments. First, we use 183 moments
21:838:66287:16:17:71:535:136:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for the pre-PPS payment schedule, reecting the daily discharge andmortality risks within the
21:839:66380:17:18:71:536:119:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:61-day window around the SSO threshold. One set of moments is associated with discharge rates
21:840:66473:18:17:80:535:101:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:20While having a discount factor so close to 1 might generally create convergence issues, in our Bellman equation
21:841:66586:17:13:71:537:84:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f13:the probability of survival enters jointly with the discount factor so that the e¤ective discount factor-(1-m(h))
21:842:66701:13:0:71:151:71:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:is not too close to 1.
22:843:66725:0:17:71:536:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:toD, another with discharge rates toU, and a third with mortality rates.  We then construct
22:844:66816:17:16:71:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:another set of 183 corresponding moments for the PPS period. Because much of the identication
22:845:66910:16:16:71:535:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is driven by the sharp change to discharge incentives at the SSO threshold, we assign greater weights
22:846:67011:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to moments that are closer to day zero (the SSO threshold) by making the weights decrease by
22:847:67103:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a constant amount (1/61) per day away from the threshold.  The second set ofmoments uses
22:848:67191:17:16:71:537:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the data on post-discharge payments to allow us to capture spillover e¤ects in our counterfactuals.
22:849:67290:16:16:71:534:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Specically, we average post-discharge payments for each discharge destination (UorD), separately
22:850:67387:16:17:72:534:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for discharges before and after the SSO threshold. We then match themto the model prediction
22:851:67479:17:16:72:534:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:regarding the health status distribution of discharged patients, thus allowing us to link health and
22:852:67579:16:16:72:195:561:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:post-discharge payments.21
22:853:67605:16:16:88:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Generating the model predictions requires us to solve the dynamic problem described in the
22:854:67695:16:17:71:534:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:previous section for each set of parameters. To ease with computation, we approximate the health
22:855:67791:17:16:71:536:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:processF(-jhi;t-1)with a discrete health space that evolves according to a Markov transition ma-
22:856:67887:16:16:71:535:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:trix (Tauchen, 1986).22This eases the solution of the dynamic problem, and at the same time
22:857:67978:16:16:71:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:allows us to read the discharge probabilities directly o¤ the solution, without any need to integrate
22:858:68079:16:20:71:429:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(presumably by simulation) over potentially intractable integration regions.
22:859:68155:20:16:88:537:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Intuition for Identication.To see the intuition for the identication of the parameters,
22:860:68244:16:16:71:537:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:it is easiest to consider rst a case where health is homogeneous across patients and over time.
22:861:68340:16:16:71:536:412:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Under this assumption, the data can be characterized by daily observations of discharge shares
22:862:68434:16:17:71:532:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:toUand toD(sUtandsDt, respectively), with the remaining patients staying at the LTCH
22:863:68518:17:16:71:535:379:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(slt= 1-sUt-sDt). The problem resembles a repeated static discrete choice problem, where the
22:864:68610:16:16:71:535:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mean utility of each discharge destination is given by the continuation valuesVUt,VDt, andVlt. As
22:865:68707:16:16:71:535:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is usual in multinomial logit models, the observed daily shares can then be inverted to recover the
22:866:68806:16:17:71:449:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:values ofVUt,VDt, andVlt, subject to a required level and scale normalizations.
22:867:68885:17:16:88:537:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Let us start with the level normalization, and consider discharge shares after the SSO threshold,
22:868:68982:16:16:71:535:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where the model is in a steady state. SettingVUt= 0allows us to estimateVDfrom the average
22:869:69072:16:16:71:535:282:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:values ofVDtandVlt, up to a scale normalization. To identify the scale parameter--, consider
22:870:69164:16:17:71:534:266:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the discharge shares the day before the SSO threshold: the jump in payments introduces a sharp
22:871:69258:17:16:71:534:249:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:change in the continuation values, which identies the e¤ect of payments onVs, or equivalently
22:872:69353:16:16:72:535:233:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:identies--. Finally, the cost parameter\ris identied o¤ the non-stationarity of the model prior
22:873:69450:16:16:71:535:217:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to the SSO threshold, and the way discharge patterns are changing as the SSO threshold gets
22:874:69541:16:17:71:536:201:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:closer. As the SSO threshold nears, the expected (remaining) lengthof stay of patients shortens,
22:875:69637:17:16:71:536:184:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:making the expected life-time costs associated with a given patientgradually decline in a way that
22:876:69735:16:16:71:537:168:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is not proportional to expected payments, allowing us to separately identify the impact of cost.
22:877:69831:16:19:71:534:152:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Loosely,Vltvaries over time as a function of the payment schedulep(t), reported costs scaled by
22:878:69926:19:17:80:537:133:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:21Because there is no variation in payments in the pre-PPS period, we do not have the variation in dischargees
22:879:70037:17:13:71:534:116:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:health that we need to identify the pre-PPS post-discharge payments model. Since we focus on the PPS period in
22:880:70147:13:10:71:537:103:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:our counterfactuals, and therefore do not need these estimates, we do not estimate pre-PPS post discharge payments.
22:881:70262:10:17:80:536:93:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:22In particular, we approximate the health distribution witha grid of 250 evenly spaced values that span a range
22:882:70374:17:0:72:413:76:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:of +/- three standard deviations around the mean of the steady state health process.
23:883:70460:0:17:71:537:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:parameter\r, and the relevant time horizon, which depends on the mortality rate and subsequent,
23:884:70555:17:16:72:539:691:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f7:endogenous discharge decisions. So one can think of the identication ofthe scale parameter--
23:885:70648:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and\ras a projection of the values ofVlton these observables. The sharp change in payments
23:886:70738:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at the SSO threshold provides a sharp change in the present value of payments and identies the
23:887:70833:16:17:71:530:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:scale parameter (or equivalently the coe¢cient on payments when thevariance of the error term
23:888:70926:17:16:71:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is standardized), and the di¤erential change in the present value ofpayments versus costs as the
23:889:71022:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patient approaches the SSO threshold identies\r. This identication can be achieved from the
23:890:71115:16:17:71:534:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS moments alone, but given that we restrict these parameters to be time-invariant, it is also
23:891:71210:17:16:71:449:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:aided by variation in discharge patterns between the pre-PPS and PPS periods.
23:892:71287:16:16:88:537:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:If health statushwere observed, we could make the argument above conditional on health,
23:893:71374:16:16:72:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and thus identify each object as a non-parametric function ofh. In practicehis unobserved, but
23:894:71468:16:17:72:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:identifying the health process is conceptually easy given our assumptions. If there are no discharges,
23:895:71570:17:16:72:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:which is roughly the case during the rst week or so of the LTCH stay, the only attrition from the
23:896:71668:16:16:72:533:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:sample is due to mortality. With only ve parameters that determine the initial health distribution
23:897:71767:16:16:72:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and how it evolves from day to day, mortality rates over ve days are su¢cient to identify the
23:898:71861:16:17:72:534:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:health process parameters, separately in the pre-PPS and PPS periods.  Once the unobserved
23:899:71951:17:16:72:534:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:health distribution is identied, we can integrate overhand apply a similar intuition to the one
23:900:72047:16:16:72:535:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we described above for the homogenoushcase. Moreover, once the health process is identied, the
23:901:72142:16:16:72:535:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cross-sectional distribution ofhvaries over time in known ways, so we can also identify how the
23:902:72239:16:17:71:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:key parameters  in particular theVs  vary as a function ofh. Finally, the relationship between
23:903:72336:17:16:72:536:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the health statushand the post-discharge payments is directly identied o¤ the data giventhat
23:904:72429:16:16:72:373:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the model does not impose any restrictions on this relationship.
23:905:72493:16:16:88:536:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Finally, the parameters of the post-discharge payments model (-0;D; -1;D; -0;U; -1;U) are identi-
23:906:72590:16:17:71:536:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ed by the sharp change in health of patients discharged on di¤erent sides of the SSO threshold.
23:907:72686:17:16:71:535:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:That is, they are essentially mapping the models prediction regarding the average health status
23:908:72782:16:16:71:537:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of dischargees before and after the SSO threshold to the corresponding average of observed post-
23:909:72878:16:16:71:534:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge payments, which also exhibit a sharp change around the SSO threshold (as shown in

24:949:76008:16:16:71:534:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the time periods, they capture not only di¤erences in the health of admitted patients but may also
24:950:76106:16:17:71:535:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reect other factors that vary over time, such as changes in medical technology or the administrative
24:951:76207:17:16:71:170:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:capacity of providers.
24:952:76229:16:16:88:535:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The model ts the data reasonably well.  Figure 8 presents our discharge moments and the
24:953:76317:16:16:71:534:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:simulated moments from the estimated model. The left column shows values in the PPS period
24:954:76407:16:17:71:535:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and the right column shows values in the pre-PPS period. The top row shows the share of discharges

24:956:76598:16:16:72:534:171:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the bottom row shows the share of patients who die at the LTCH. The model does a very good job
24:957:76692:16:17:72:535:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:tting the spike in discharges toUandDin the PPS period. This is particularly encouraging
24:958:76783:17:16:72:534:138:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:because this variation is our key source of identifying variation. Themodel t for the mortality
24:959:76879:16:16:72:535:122:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patterns in the pre-PPS and PPS periods is good over the initial days, but less good at longer time
24:960:76978:16:16:72:535:106:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:horizons. This is likely due to our fairly parsimonious parameterizationof the health process. The
24:961:77076:16:17:72:385:90:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:model t is also poorer for discharges toUin the pre-PPS period.
24:962:77140:17:0:88:534:73:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 9 provides some intuition for how the model operates, illustrating the policy function
25:963:77237:0:17:71:536:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at the estimated parameters.23Healthy patients (higherh) are discharged toD, while sick patients
25:964:77333:17:16:71:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(lowerh) are discharged toU. Consistent with the descriptive evidence, LTCHs work down the
25:965:77424:16:16:72:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:distribution at the jump and lower their discharge threshold on theDmargin and conversely work
25:966:77519:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:up the distribution on theUmargin and increase the discharge threshold. The larger shift on
25:967:77612:16:17:72:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:theDmargin relative to theUmargin relates directly to our discussion above on the magnitude
25:968:77703:17:16:72:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the slope parameter estimates (v1;Dandv1;U). The relatively small outward shift in the policy
25:969:77799:16:16:71:534:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:function just before the SSO threshold is consistent with the descriptive results which show limited
25:970:77899:16:32:71:420:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evidence on missing mass immediately to the left of the SSO threshold.
25:971:77971:32:24:71:379:562:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:4.4  The e¤ects of counterfactual nancial incentives
25:972:78024:24:16:71:536:538:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use our model to simulate discharge patterns and Medicare payments under a variety of coun-
25:973:78118:16:16:71:536:522:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:terfactual payment schedules. Throughout this section, we assume that the initial distribution of
25:974:78215:16:16:71:535:506:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:health of admitted patients stays the same but that the subsequent discharge decisions reect the
25:975:78312:16:17:71:360:490:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:incentives provided by the counterfactual payment schedules.
25:976:78372:17:16:88:533:473:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We limit our attention to alternative schedules that maintain the current practice of a cap
25:977:78463:16:16:71:536:457:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on payments after a certain number of days.  We do this both because it respects the current
25:978:78555:16:16:71:535:441:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:policy approach toward LTCH payments and because an uncapped schedule would lead to a
25:979:78642:16:17:71:536:425:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:small number of long hospitals stays, which is outside of the variation in our data. Specically,
25:980:78739:17:16:71:534:408:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we consider three main types of counterfactuals: payment schedules that remove the jump while
25:981:78832:16:16:71:535:392:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:holding the threshold day constant, payment schedules that eliminate the jump (and allow the
25:982:78924:16:16:71:536:376:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold day to vary) while holding LTCHs harmless, and cost-based reimbursement at a constant
25:983:79019:16:20:71:358:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:per diem, capped at 60 days. We discuss each in turn below.
25:984:79078:20:16:88:537:340:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Removing the jump.We start by considering two simple modications of the baseline pay-
25:985:79164:16:16:72:535:324:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ment schedule that eliminate the jump in payments at the SSO threshold, but, like the baseline
25:986:79258:16:16:72:537:308:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS payment schedule, provide no payments on the margin for stays in excess of the SSO threshold.
25:987:79355:16:17:72:537:292:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 10 plots these counterfactual payment schedules and the baseline schedule for comparison.
25:988:79451:17:16:72:534:275:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The top panel shows a counterfactual we call higher rate per day, whicheliminates the jump by
25:989:79546:16:16:72:535:259:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increasing the per-diem payment from $1,384 to $2,151 prior to the SSO threshold but holds the
25:990:79640:16:16:72:535:243:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-threshold payment xed. The bottom panel shows a counterfactual schedule we call lower
25:991:79732:16:17:72:535:227:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cap, which eliminates the jump in payments at the SSO threshold byreducing the PPS payment

25:994:80008:16:17:72:180:178:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is weakly less generous.
25:995:80032:17:16:88:535:161:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use our model to simulate discharge patterns and Medicare payments under these two
25:996:80117:16:16:72:536:145:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:counterfactuals. Figure 11 shows the policy functions under each payment schedule, Figure 12 shows

25:998:80311:18:17:80:533:111:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:23The policy function is not a deterministic function ofh; given the-s in the LTCHs keep and discharge ow
25:999:80419:17:13:71:536:94:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:payo¤ functions (see equations 1 and 2) ,his related to discharge stochastically. The policy lines inFigure 9 are
25:1000:80532:13:0:72:477:81:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:drawn so that at that given level ofh, 50% of the patients are discharged to the relevant destination.
26:1001:80636:0:17:71:335:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare payments to the LTCH and to other facilities.
26:1002:80690:17:16:88:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The black dashed lines in Figure 11 show the policy function under thehigher rate per day
26:1003:80781:16:16:71:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:counterfactual. During the rst few days, the policy function is similar to that under the observed
26:1004:80880:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule. However, as the length-of-stay increases, the elimination of the jump reduces the incentive
26:1005:80981:16:17:71:537:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to retain patients, and the policy function shifts inwards on the upstream and downstream margins.
26:1006:81079:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The black dashed lines in Figure 12 shows discharge patterns underthis counterfactual. Mirroring
26:1007:81175:16:16:71:534:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the changes in the policy function, the higher rate per day counterfactual increases discharges in
26:1008:81275:16:17:71:535:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the 10 days prior to the SSO threshold, relative to the observed schedule. As shown in column (2)
26:1009:81372:17:16:71:505:577:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:of Table 4, these higher discharge rates reduce average length of stay by 1.9 days or 7.2%.24
26:1010:81465:16:16:88:537:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Despite the signicantly higher per-day payments prior to the jump ($2,151 versus $1,384),
26:1011:81555:16:16:71:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare payments to LTCHs increase by a very small amount of $369 or 1%. The small increase
26:1012:81647:16:17:71:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is due to a large behavioral response to the incentives. Holding discharge patterns xed, LTCHs
26:1013:81742:17:16:71:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:would get paid about 50% more per day for stays below the SSO threshold, butin response to the
26:1014:81836:16:16:71:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:elimination of the jump, patients are now discharged earlier, so overall payments are lower. Holding
26:1015:81936:16:16:71:534:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge patterns xed, we calculate that the mechanical e¤ect of this counterfactual is a $1,767
26:1016:82034:16:17:71:537:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increase in Medicare payments to the LTCH, implying that the behavioral response to the removal
26:1017:82129:17:16:71:535:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the jump reduces Medicare payments to the LTCH by $1,398 per admission. LTCH prots per

26:1019:82291:16:16:88:534:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We next explore the e¤ects of the lower cap payment schedule.  The grey lines in Figure

26:1030:83301:16:16:88:536:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The remaining rows of Table 4 consider the impact of these counterfactual payment schedules

26:1032:83486:16:17:72:536:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:spillovers on post-discharge payments are small. For the higher rate per day counterfactual, post-
26:1033:83586:17:16:72:535:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge payments for patients dischargedUandDare a¤ected by a few hundred dollars. For

26:1040:84267:13:0:71:89:73:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:data.
27:1041:84274:0:17:71:224:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:by a substantial $12,467 or 26%.
27:1042:84306:17:16:88:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While interesting, neither of the above counterfactuals is fully satisfactory. While the lower
27:1043:84401:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cap counterfactual suggests that alternative payment schedules couldsubstantially reduce Medicare
27:1044:84499:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments, the large decrease in LTCH revenue (and in estimated prots) might have unintended
27:1045:84591:16:17:71:537:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consequences. For instance, under this payment schedule, LTCHs might cut back on socially valu-
27:1046:84686:17:16:71:537:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:able xed investments or even exit the market. In contrast, the higher rate per day counterfactual,
27:1047:84787:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:while clearly making LTCHs better o¤, has virtually no e¤ect on Medicarepayments. Yet, these two
27:1048:84883:16:17:71:536:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:exercises suggest that there might be intermediate contracts that generate cost savings without
27:1049:84980:17:19:71:435:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the risk of unintended consequences. We explore such counterfactuals below.
27:1050:85055:19:16:88:534:558:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Win-win payment schedules. With the above considerations in mind, we now consider a
27:1051:85140:16:16:71:534:542:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:set of counterfactuals that hold LTCH revenue xed under the observed discharge patterns. Faced
27:1052:85235:16:17:71:537:526:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with these contracts, if LTCHs do not change their behavior, they will have identical revenue,
27:1053:85329:17:16:71:535:509:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:costs, and prots as they would under the observed payment schedule. If the LTCH change their
27:1054:85422:16:16:71:535:493:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:behavior, by revealed preferences, they must be (weakly) bettero¤. Therefore, by design, these
27:1055:85516:16:16:71:335:477:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:contracts should not have a negative impact on LTCHs.
27:1056:85569:16:17:88:536:461:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In the same spirit as the previous counterfactuals, we consider contracts that pay a constant
27:1057:85662:17:16:72:535:444:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:per-diem amount up to a threshold length of stay, at which point the payments are capped and per
27:1058:85758:16:16:72:535:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:diem payments drop to zero (obviously, with no jump). We considercontracts where the payment
27:1059:85850:16:16:72:535:412:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is capped at thresholds in a +/- 10 day range on either side of the currentSSO threshold day. Since
27:1060:85949:16:17:72:534:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the generosity of the contract is strictly increasing in the per diem rate, for a given length of stay
27:1061:86051:17:16:72:534:379:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at which payments are capped, there is a unique payment schedule that holds LTCH revenue xed
27:1062:86144:16:16:72:250:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:under the baseline discharge patterns.
27:1063:86182:16:16:88:536:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Figure 13 plots a set of these contracts, where to avoid overcrowding the gure, we show payment
27:1064:86278:16:17:72:531:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedules for only a subset of the 21 contracts considered. The toppanel of Figure 14 plots LTCH
27:1065:86374:17:16:72:534:314:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments against total Medicare payments (including estimated post-discharge payments) for each
27:1066:86469:16:16:72:535:298:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the contracts we consider. The bottom panel of Figure 14 plots estimated LTCH prots against
27:1067:86564:16:16:72:535:282:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare payments for each contract. For comparison, both plots also show outcomes under the
27:1068:86656:16:17:72:201:266:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:observed payment schedule.
27:1069:86682:17:16:88:535:249:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The gures indicate that there is a broad set of win-win payment schedules that reduce
27:1070:86770:16:16:72:534:233:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:total Medicare payments for the episode of care while leaving LTCHs (weakly) better o¤. Every
27:1071:86863:16:16:72:533:217:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:counterfactual contract with a threshold between -8 and 8 days reduces Medicare spending, although
27:1072:86961:16:17:72:534:201:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:there is substantial heterogeneity in the reduction. LTCH revenues increase for every contract with
27:1073:87060:17:16:72:535:184:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a threshold of 6 to 10 days and declines for contracts with a threshold of -10 to 5 days. Because
27:1074:87157:16:16:72:534:168:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs value both prots and patient utility, LTCH prots under counterfactuals do not necessarily
27:1075:87254:16:17:72:534:152:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:increase. LTCH prots are higher than their baseline level for contracts with a threshold -10 to
27:1076:87350:17:16:72:535:135:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2 days and are lower for thresholds of 3 to 10 days. Counterfactuals thatdecrease prots do not
27:1077:87445:16:16:72:244:119:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:lower them by a substantial amount.
27:1078:87480:16:0:88:535:103:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The counterfactual with payment threshold of 1 day more than the current SSO threshold results
28:1079:87576:0:17:71:536:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the largest reduction in Medicare spending and is a natural contract to focus on.25Column (4)
28:1080:87672:17:16:71:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of Table 4 shows outcomes for this contract. Under this payment schedule, Medicare payments to
28:1081:87766:16:16:71:535:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs are reduced by $1,660 or 5.9%. Accounting for Medicare payments acrossthe entire episode
28:1082:87860:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of care leads to somewhat higher savings of $2,145, or 4.5% of total episode payments. Despite the
28:1083:87958:16:17:71:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reduced payments, LTCH prots rise by $344 per stay or 5.1%: the  decline in LTCH revenue is
28:1084:88050:17:19:71:396:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:o¤set by lower costs, as length of stay is almost 2 days (9%) shorter.
28:1085:88120:19:16:88:534:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Cost-based payment schedules.The last two columns of Table 4 report results from two
28:1086:88204:16:17:71:535:591:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:additional counterfactuals that explore cost-based reimbursement as a constant per diem rate. The
28:1087:88301:17:16:71:535:574:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:rst counterfactual pays LTCHs a constant per diem of their estimated costs, which is $1,107 per
28:1088:88397:16:16:71:536:558:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:day. The second counterfactual pays LTCHs a constant per diem of $507, which is the average per
28:1089:88492:16:16:71:534:542:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:day payment to SNFs during the post-discharge period. We think about this counterfactual as a
28:1090:88585:16:17:71:535:526:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:form of reference based pricing where Medicare pays LTCHs the opportunity cost to Medicare
28:1091:88677:17:16:71:536:509:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the patient  i.e., the amount Medicare would have incurred for thepatient at a location that
28:1092:88773:16:16:71:534:493:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:provides fairly similar care (at least for the marginal patient). To avoid extrapolating too far outside
28:1093:88877:16:16:71:535:477:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of our data, for both of these counterfactuals we cap payments after 60 days, which leads LTCHs
28:1094:88972:16:17:71:483:461:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to discharge virtually all of their patients within 90 days of the current SSO threshold.
28:1095:89061:17:16:88:535:444:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Paying LTCHs their estimated costs leads to a substantial $7,010 increase in payments to LTCHs
28:1096:89155:16:16:71:532:428:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and a smaller $3,530 overall increase in total Medicare payments. Paymentsincrease because LTCH
28:1097:89250:16:16:71:535:412:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:retain patients for longer time periods rather than discharging them toSNFs where Medicare
28:1098:89340:16:17:71:534:396:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments would be lower, with average length of stay increasing from 19.2 to 32.4 days. Paying
28:1099:89434:17:16:71:537:379:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs the average per-diem for SNFs leads to a massive decrease in LTCH payments and total
28:1100:89524:16:16:71:536:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare payments, accompanied by a sharp reduction in length of stay. Of course, concerns about
28:1101:89620:16:16:71:534:347:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:unintended consequences, which we discussed in the context of the lower cap schedule, are also
28:1102:89716:16:20:71:132:331:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:relevant here.
28:1103:89730:20:16:88:535:311:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:For-prot versus non-prot LTCHs.At the end of Section 3, we presented descriptive
28:1104:89812:16:16:72:537:295:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evidence where we split the sample by whether the LTCH was a for-prot or non-prot hospital.
28:1105:89905:16:16:72:536:279:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In particular, we showed that while payment schedules were similarfor for-prot and non-prot
28:1106:89998:16:17:72:536:263:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs, for-prots exhibited a larger increase in discharges at theSSO threshold, suggesting that
28:1107:90094:17:16:72:535:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:these facilities are more responsive to nancial incentives. Our model allows us to quantify the
28:1108:90190:16:16:72:536:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:magnitude of the di¤erences in for-prot and non-prot behavior, and to adjust for potential di¤er-
28:1109:90289:16:16:72:534:214:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ences in the health of the patients admitted to these facilities to more precisely isolate heterogeneity
28:1110:90393:16:17:72:215:198:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in their response to incentives.
28:1111:90425:17:16:88:535:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:To conduct this analysis, we estimate the model allowing all of the parameters except those
28:1112:90516:16:16:72:536:165:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:governing post-discharge payments to vary by for-prot status. We forcethe post-discharge pay-
28:1113:90610:16:17:72:536:149:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ments parameters to be the same because, conditional onhat discharge, post-discharge payments
28:1114:90703:17:16:72:537:132:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:should not depend on whether the patient was discharged from a for-prot ornon-prot hospital.
28:1115:90797:16:18:72:535:116:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In addition, requiring the parameters to be equal helps ensure that the health process has the same
28:1116:90896:18:17:80:537:98:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:25By contrast, we achieve the highest Medicare dissavings with the counterfactual with the payment threshold of
28:1117:91007:17:0:71:280:81:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:-10 days, where Medicare spending rises by over 2%.
29:1118:91060:0:17:71:361:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:cardinal interpretation across these di¤erent types of LTCHs.
29:1119:91121:17:16:88:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Table A4 shows the parameter estimates. The health process parameters are broadly
29:1120:91211:16:16:71:536:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:similar across the two types of hospitals. The point estimate for costs is somewhat lower for for-
29:1121:91309:16:16:71:537:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:prot (\r= 0:81) than non-prot (\r= 0:91) hospitals, which is consistent with higher operational
29:1122:91406:16:17:71:534:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:e¢ciency by the private sector. The point estimates on the slope parameters (v1;Uandv1;D) are also
29:1123:91504:17:16:72:535:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:smaller in absolute value for for-prots than non-prots, indicating thatfor-prots are more elastic
29:1124:91604:16:16:72:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to nancial incentives in their discharge decisions. We note, however, that the non-prot parameters
29:1125:91704:16:17:72:515:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:are less precisely estimated so that the di¤erences are only sometimes statistically signicant.
29:1126:91800:17:16:88:537:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Comparing for-prots and non-prots parameter by parameter is not particularly insightful.
29:1127:91890:16:16:72:536:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Therefore, to quantify the di¤erence between for- and non-prots over the full set of parameters,
29:1128:91987:16:16:72:536:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Table 5 conducts the counterfactual of assigning every LTCH for-prot versus non-prot status.
29:1129:92081:16:17:72:535:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:For reference, column (1) shows outcomes under the observed organizational form. To construct
29:1130:92174:17:16:72:535:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:column (2), we assign all LTCHs the\r,-1;U,-0;D,-1;D, and--estimates for for-prot hospitals
29:1131:92266:16:16:71:533:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:but maintain the heterogenous health process parameters so that thereis no change in the health
29:1132:92363:16:16:71:540:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:distribution of the patients. For column (3), we assign all LTCHs the non-prot estimates for\r,
29:1133:92459:16:17:72:346:464:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:-1;U,-0;D,-1;D, and--and hold the health process xed.
29:1134:92513:17:16:88:536:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Consistent with the increased responsiveness to incentives, payments to LTCHs are $458 (or
29:1135:92603:16:16:71:535:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:about 1.7%) higher under the all for-prot than the all non-prot counterfactual. We estimate
29:1136:92700:16:16:71:536:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that LTCH prots are more than twice as large ($5,681 versus $2,590) under the counterfactual
29:1137:92793:16:17:71:536:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where they are all for-prot. Interestingly, the $458 higher payments only accounts for about one-
29:1138:92891:17:16:71:537:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:fth of the higher prots, with the majority of the di¤erence being dueto lower estimated costs.
29:1139:92987:16:16:71:536:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Accounting for post-discharge payments, the estimates imply that the counterfactual of convert-
29:1140:93083:16:16:71:536:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ing all LTCHs from for-prot to non-prot status would reduce total Medicare payments across
29:1141:93176:16:17:71:534:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the entire episode of care by $1,376 or about 2.9% of their current level, holding the distribution
29:1142:93275:17:19:71:177:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of patient health xed.
29:1143:93298:19:16:88:535:298:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:Robustness.In our baseline model, we made a number of parametric assumptions. In order
29:1144:93384:16:16:71:535:282:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to assess the sensitivity of our main results to these assumptions, Table 6reports the main results
29:1145:93483:16:17:71:535:266:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:from a subset of the (many) alternative specications that we examined.The results appear to be
29:1146:93578:17:16:71:164:249:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:qualitatively robust.
29:1147:93599:16:16:88:534:233:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In our rst alternative specication, we relax the assumption that the health process is stationary
29:1148:93698:16:16:71:539:217:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f6:by allowing the auto-correlation parameter-to vary with length-of-stay according to-=-0+
29:1149:93786:16:17:72:535:201:11:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f3:-1ln(t+ 1), where the time index is dened such that the patient is admitted on datet= 0:26
29:1150:93877:17:16:72:534:184:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:While in the pre-PPS period-1is very close to zero, in the PPS period the estimate is slightly
29:1151:93971:16:16:71:535:168:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:negative-1=-0:005, which is consistent with health becoming less stable over the course of the
29:1152:94065:16:16:72:535:152:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stay.  However, as shown in Panel B of Table 6, enriching the specication in this manner has
29:1153:94158:16:17:72:534:136:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:virtually no e¤ect on the counterfactuals. We also specied other models of health processes, such
29:1154:94256:17:18:72:534:119:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:as a random walk and a random walk with a drift, but the ability of these models to t the data
29:1155:94351:18:17:80:536:101:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:26We assume that the correlation parameter becomes xed after45 days at a value of-=-0 +-1ln(46)so that
29:1156:94454:17:13:72:536:84:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:the dynamic programming problem becomes stationary fort >45, allowing us to solve thet >45problem by value
29:1157:94560:13:0:72:319:71:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:function iteration and earlier periods by backwards induction.
30:1158:94624:0:17:71:296:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:was much worse than our baseline specication.
30:1159:94670:17:16:88:535:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The second specication reported in Table 6 ts the model using onlythe PPS moments. The
30:1160:94758:16:16:71:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:counterfactuals, shown in Panel C of Table 3, are very similar to the baseline estimates, suggesting
30:1161:94858:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that the sharp jump in payments at the SSO threshold, relative to over-time variation from the
30:1162:94952:16:17:71:536:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:implementation of LTCH-PPS, is the key driver of the results. The limited importance of the over-
30:1163:95049:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:time variation presumably stems from the fact that we allow distinct health process parameters in
30:1164:95146:16:38:71:377:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:each period, thereby soaking up much of the over-time variation.
30:1165:95210:38:27:71:172:572:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:5  Conclusions
30:1166:95224:27:16:71:537:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In this paper, we examined the impact of provider nancial incentives in post-acute care (PAC),
30:1167:95319:16:17:71:534:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a setting with large stakes both for the government budget and patient health that has received
30:1168:95414:17:16:71:537:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:scant attention in the academic literature. Within the context of PAC,we examined the impact of
30:1169:95509:16:16:71:534:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a jump in Medicare payments to long term care hospitals (LTCHs) that occurs after a pre-specied
30:1170:95605:16:16:71:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:length of stay, when reimbursement shifts from a per diem rate to a lump sum payment. At the
30:1171:95697:16:17:71:534:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold, the payment for keeping an individual another day jumps byabout $13,000, with no
30:1172:95788:17:16:71:355:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:marginal payment for additional days beyond the threshold.
30:1173:95846:16:16:88:535:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The descriptive evidence shows a large response by LTCHs to the jump in payments. At the
30:1174:95934:16:16:72:536:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold, there is a large spike in discharges.  The marginal patient a¤ected by the payment
30:1175:96027:16:17:72:535:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold is relatively healthy. We are unable to detect any impacton patient mortality at the
30:1176:96121:17:16:72:305:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold, even in this high-mortality population.
30:1177:96171:16:16:88:534:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:To examine the implications of alternative payment schedules, we specied and estimated a
30:1178:96260:16:16:71:537:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stylized dynamic model of LTCH discharge behavior which allows us to model LTCH behavior,
30:1179:96349:16:17:71:535:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments, and prots, as well as total Medicare payments for the episodeof care, under alternative
30:1180:96447:17:16:71:534:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment schedules.  We used the estimated model to search for win-win contracts that hold
30:1181:96538:16:16:71:535:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCHs (and presumably its patients as well) harmless, while reducingMedicare payments. The
30:1182:96628:16:16:71:535:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:contract with the largest Medicare savings reduced total Medicare payments by 5% while increasing
30:1183:96725:16:17:71:249:269:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH prots by a similar percentage.
30:1184:96761:17:16:88:535:252:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We also considered more aggressive payment schedules that resulted in substantially higher
30:1185:96851:16:16:71:533:236:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Medicare savings but raised the possibility of unintended consequences due to a large reduction in
30:1186:96949:16:16:71:535:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:LTCH prots. In particular, in our model we take admission to the LTCH asgiven and focus on the
30:1187:97044:16:17:71:535:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:impact of counterfactual policies on length of stay and payments to theLTCH and post-discharge
30:1188:97138:17:16:71:535:187:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:costs for this xed set of patients. However, the large reduction inprots brought about in our
30:1189:97233:16:16:71:536:171:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:more aggressive counterfactuals may a¤ect which patients are admitted to anLTCH and might
30:1190:97322:16:17:71:536:155:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:have even broader e¤ects on the market, for instance through entry and exit decisions. We consider
30:1191:97420:17:16:71:534:138:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an important area for further work to move up the healthcare pathway and model the decision
30:1192:97513:16:16:71:438:122:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to discharge from an acute hospital stay to an LTCH or other PAC facilities.
30:1193:97589:16:16:88:536:106:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:More broadly, our results indicate how economic models and data can be combined to better
30:1194:97678:16:17:71:535:90:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:inform contract design. Little other than common sense was needed to see thatsomealternative
30:1195:97770:17:0:72:535:73:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment schedule should be better for both the Medicare payer and theLTCH. Data and descriptive
31:1196:97867:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:evidence, however, were important to move beyond this conceptual existence proof to demonstrate
31:1197:97964:17:16:71:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a quantitatively meaningful behavioral response by the LTCH to the current payment schedule. An
31:1198:98059:16:16:71:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:economic model and its estimates were necessary to identify specic contracts that could create a
31:1199:98156:16:16:71:535:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:win-win for the LTCH and Medicare payments given the LTCHs optimizing behavioral response
31:1200:98248:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:to alternative contracts. While naturally our results are specic to our particular setting, we hope
31:1201:98348:17:16:71:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that this type of approach can inform future work examining the impact of provider nancial
31:1202:98439:16:38:71:505:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:incentives not only for the directly a¤ected provider but throughout the healthcare system.

33:1258:102339:38:27:71:403:663:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:Appendix A. Post-discharge payments and days
33:1259:102383:27:16:71:535:636:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Our starting point of the analysis is an admission to an LTCH. We can observe all discharge
33:1260:102474:16:16:71:536:620:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:destinations from an LTCH. Appendix Table A1 shows the share of discharges todi¤erent locations.
33:1261:102570:16:16:71:534:604:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In the PPS period, 14% of patients die during their LTCH stay, another 11% are discharged
33:1262:102659:16:17:71:534:588:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:upstream (with the vast majority going to inpatient care) and 75% are discharged downstream
33:1263:102754:17:16:71:534:571:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(with approximately half of these patients going to a SNF/IRF and 40% going home, where they
33:1264:102846:16:16:71:223:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:may receive care from an HHA).
33:1265:102876:16:16:88:534:539:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We dene a post-discharge episode of care as the spell of almost continuous days following
33:1266:102968:16:17:71:537:523:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:discharge from an LTCH with Medicare payments to an ACH, SNF/IRF or LTCH. In particular,
33:1267:103056:17:16:71:526:506:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the episode ends if there are at least two days without Medicare payments to these institutions.
33:1268:103152:16:16:88:535:490:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Although we can observe all discharge destinations within the episode of care, we only observe
33:1269:103246:16:16:71:535:474:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:claim-level Medicare payments and days at ACHs, SNF/IRFs, and LTCHs. To addressthe fact
33:1270:103333:16:17:71:534:458:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that we do not observe payments or days at HHAs or hospices, we supplement theMedPAR data
33:1271:103422:17:16:71:534:441:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with annual spending and utilization from the Beneciary Summary File (BSF) Cost & Utilization
33:1272:103516:16:16:71:534:425:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:le. For every stay in the MedPAR data, we observe whether the patient was discharged to an
33:1273:103607:16:16:71:531:409:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:HHA or hospice at some point in the episode of care. For patients who were discharged to an HHA
33:1274:103702:16:17:71:466:393:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:or hospice, we impute the patients payments and days using the annual BSFdata.
33:1275:103781:17:16:88:535:376:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Since these annual amounts include some payments and days that occur before or after the
33:1276:103869:16:16:71:536:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:episode of care, our imputation likely leads us to overestimate post-discharge Medicare payments
33:1277:103967:16:17:71:534:344:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and days. However, we think that the approach provides a reasonable approximation. Appendix
33:1278:104058:17:16:71:534:327:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Table A2 shows that our estimates of post-discharge payments and facility days are not a¤ected
33:1279:104152:16:38:71:451:311:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:much if we instead impute 0 costs for HHA and hospice, and 0 days for hospice.
33:1280:104230:38:27:71:330:273:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:Appendix B. LTCH payment systems
33:1281:104262:27:16:71:535:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Prior to scal year 2003 (i.e. October 2002), CMS reimbursed LTCHs on a cost-based system. At
33:1282:104355:16:16:71:531:230:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the start of scal year 2003, CMS began transitioning LTCHs to a prospective payment system
33:1283:104446:16:17:71:535:214:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(PPS). The PPS, which was fully phased in by the start of scal year 2008 (i.e. October 2007), is
33:1284:104543:17:16:71:537:197:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the focus of our study. This appendix describes it in more detail, drawing heavily on Kim et al.
33:1285:104639:16:32:71:451:181:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:(2015), Medicare Reimbursement Reference Guide (2015), and MedPAC (2014).
33:1286:104712:32:23:71:195:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B.1. LTCH PPS rules
33:1287:104731:23:16:71:536:126:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In contrast to the cost-based system, which had reimbursed hospitals based on the estimated cost of
33:1288:104830:16:17:71:535:110:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:each patients case, the PPS outlined a xed reimbursement amount for each patient, based on the
33:1289:104926:17:16:71:535:93:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:patients DRG. These DRG-based lump-sum payments were meant to reect the typical resources
33:1290:105017:16:0:71:533:77:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:consumed by each type of patient. However, in order to discourage short stays in hospitals which
34:1291:105115:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:were meant to provide long-term care, the PPS includes a short stay outlier (SSO) threshold, with
34:1292:105212:17:16:71:534:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reduced payments below the full DRG payment for LTCH patients whoare discharged before a
34:1293:105300:16:31:71:182:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:DRG-specic threshold.
34:1294:105322:31:24:71:171:644:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Full DRG payment
34:1295:105338:24:32:71:252:620:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The full DRG payment is computed as
34:1296:105373:32:23:138:535:588:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Full DRG Payment = Adjusted Federal Rate x DRG Relative Weight,(11)
34:1297:105440:23:27:71:94:565:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:where
34:1298:105445:27:20:79:512:538:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Adjusted Federal Rate  =  (Unadj. Federal Rate x Labor-Related Share x Wage Index) +
34:1299:105529:20:27:218:535:518:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:+ (Unadj. Federal Rate x Nonlabor-Related Share).(12)
34:1300:105582:27:16:88:535:491:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:This payment structure is similar to the much-studied Inpatient PPS used for (regular) acute
34:1301:105674:16:16:71:535:475:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:care hospitals (ACHs) that was introduced in 1983, but di¤ers in two ways.First, although the
34:1302:105767:16:17:71:533:459:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:DRGs are dened in the same way for the LTCH and Inpatient PPS, the relative weights associated
34:1303:105862:17:16:71:536:442:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with DRGs have di¤erent values in LTCH-PPS. Second, the LTCH-PPS unadjusted federal rate is
34:1304:105953:16:16:71:534:426:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:larger than the corresponding Inpatient PPS value. The result is that LTCH-PPS payments are
34:1305:106044:16:16:71:535:410:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:substantially greater than Inpatient PPS payments for the same DRG, presumably to reect the
34:1306:106136:16:32:71:296:394:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:greater costs at an LTCH relative to an ACH.27
34:1307:106182:32:23:71:251:362:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Short stay outlier (SSO) payment
34:1308:106214:23:16:71:535:339:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:If an LTCH stay has a length of stay (LOS) shorter than or equal to ve-sixths of the geometric
34:1309:106309:16:17:71:535:323:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:average length of stay (ALOS) for the DRG, it is paid as a short stay outlier. We call the smallest
34:1310:106408:17:16:71:535:306:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:integer greater than ve-sixth of the geometric ALOS the SSO Threshold.The SSO threshold is
34:1311:106499:16:16:71:398:290:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:constant within a DRG-PPS Rate Year (with the exception of 2009).
34:1312:106564:16:28:88:347:274:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:A short stay outlier is paid thelowestof the following:
34:1313:106619:28:26:85:191:246:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1. Full DRG Payment.
34:1314:106639:26:16:85:536:220:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:2. 120% of the DRG per diem amount times the length of stay, where the DRG perdiem amount
34:1315:106728:16:18:99:536:204:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is dened as the ratio of the full DRG payment to the geometric average ofthe LOS for that
34:1316:106818:18:17:80:537:186:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:27Also, like Inpatient PPS, LTCH PPS o¤ers a High Cost Outlier (HCO) payment for  particularly costly stays.
34:1317:106926:17:13:71:532:169:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:Specically, an LTCH can receive a HCO payment if the cost of the case exceeds the HCO Threshold. The HCO
34:1318:107031:13:13:71:535:156:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:payment is made in addition to the regular payment amount. Importantly, for our purposes, HCO payments can be
34:1319:107140:13:13:71:535:143:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:made regardless of whether the LTCH stay is considered an SSOoutlier or eligible for the full DRG payment. We
34:1320:107249:13:14:71:536:130:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:therefore exclude HCO payments from our analysis and model.About 9% of LTCH stays in our baseline sample have
34:1321:107358:14:0:71:399:116:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:HCO payments, and the median HCO payment in our baseline sample is $12,428.
35:1322:107435:0:17:99:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:DRG. This option is roughly equivalent to a linear interpolation of the full DRG payment
35:1323:107523:17:25:99:292:691:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:between Day 0 and the SSO Threshold.28
35:1324:107561:25:16:85:536:666:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:3. 100% of the cost of the case, which is computed as total charges multiplied by the facility-
35:1325:107656:16:25:99:229:650:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:specic cost-to-charge ratio.
35:1326:107685:25:17:85:537:625:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:4. A blend of the Inpatient PPS amount (used at ACH) and 120% of the DRG per diem amount.
35:1327:107774:17:32:99:390:608:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Note that this option converges to option 2 as LOS increases.
35:1328:107835:32:23:71:266:576:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B.2. Empirical payment schedules
35:1329:107867:23:23:71:191:553:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:PPS payment schedule
35:1330:107887:23:17:71:534:530:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We use a commercial software o¤ered by the company 3M (the product is called Core Grouping
35:1331:107978:17:16:71:535:513:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:Software (CGS)) to compute counterfactual Medicare payments for eachpost-PPS period stay.29
35:1332:108070:16:16:72:535:497:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Specically, for each stay in the PPS period, we compute the PPS payment for the actual discharge
35:1333:108167:16:16:72:536:481:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:day and each possible counterfactual discharge day. The inputs into this calculation are the ad-
35:1334:108263:16:17:72:535:465:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mission date, estimated hospital charges, principal and secondary diagnoses, procedures, discharge
35:1335:108361:17:16:72:536:448:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:status, age, and sex of the patient. For counterfactual lengths of stay, we assume that hospital
35:1336:108457:16:16:72:329:432:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:charges scale linearly with the observed length of stay.
35:1337:108513:16:16:88:534:416:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:With this information, the software produces the DRG code, the SSO threshold day, and the
35:1338:108602:16:17:71:534:400:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:total Medicare payment for each length of stay. To validate the software, we compare the predicted
35:1339:108700:17:16:71:534:383:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:DRG against the DRG we observe in the data, and the predicted payment against the observed
35:1340:108790:16:16:71:531:367:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment for the observed length of stay. The predicted DRG matches the observed value in 99.9%
35:1341:108884:16:16:71:536:351:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:stays and the predicted Medicare payment is within one dollar of the observed Medicare payment
35:1342:108978:16:17:71:144:335:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in 90% of stays.
35:1343:108994:17:16:88:534:318:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Figure A1 illustrates the resultant, estimated payment schedules for both the pre-PPS
35:1344:109088:16:16:71:535:302:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and PPS periods. Note that this gure di¤ers slightly from Figure 1 in the paper, which depicts
35:1345:109183:16:16:71:535:286:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:a stylized model of the post-period payment schedules in which thepre-threshold payments are
35:1346:109275:16:17:71:537:270:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:constant per diem. In practice, the pre-threshold payments appear tobe slightly bowed downwards;
35:1347:109371:17:16:71:535:253:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we abstract from this in Figure 1 which we use in our model estimates, where we used the average
35:1348:109467:16:16:71:535:237:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payment per day for stays discharged before the threshold to construct the slope of the payment
35:1349:109562:16:16:71:218:221:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule prior to the threshold.
35:1350:109594:16:17:88:536:205:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:What features of the payment rule created the jump in payments at theSSO threshold? Recall
35:1351:109684:17:16:71:535:188:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:that right of the SSO Threshold, short-stay outlier rules do not applyand the payment is just the
35:1352:109781:16:16:71:536:172:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:full DRG payment, which means the cumulative payment schedule is always a at line to the right
35:1353:109876:16:18:71:535:156:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the threshold. To the left of the SSO Threshold, each stay is paid the minimum of four alternative
35:1354:109977:18:17:80:532:138:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:28To see this, note that 120% of the DRG per diem amount times thelength of stay is approximately equal to 120%
35:1355:110088:17:14:71:536:121:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:x (Full DRG payment) / ((6/5)SSO Threshold)) x LOS, which is equal to (Full DRG payment) / (SSO Threshold)
35:1356:110194:14:10:71:97:107:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:x LOS.
35:1357:110200:10:17:80:536:97:6:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:29For more information about this software,  see:  http://solutions.3m.com/wps/portal/3M/en_US/Health-
35:1358:110302:17:0:72:449:80:9:g_d0_f3:9:g_d0_f3:Information-Systems/HIS/Products-and-Services/Products-List-A-Z/Core-Grouping-Software/
36:1359:110391:0:17:71:535:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:payments; the shape of the payment schedules therefore depends onwhich of the four alternatives
36:1360:110486:17:16:71:537:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:is binding. If options 1, 2, or 4 were binding, we would not see a jump at the threshold. Therefore,
36:1361:110586:16:16:71:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:we conclude that cost of the case must be binding in most cases because we observe a jump on
36:1362:110678:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:average. Note that the cost of case being binding is necessary rather than the su¢cient condition
36:1363:110775:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:for creating a jump in the payment schedule; the costs could theoretically be such that the payment
36:1364:110874:17:16:71:536:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:schedule only has a kink at the SSO threshold rather than a jump. In practice, however, the cost
36:1365:110970:16:32:71:502:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:of the case is on average lower than the other options, and we see a jump at the threshold.
36:1366:111061:32:23:71:214:578:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Pre-PPS payment schedule
36:1367:111085:23:16:71:534:555:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In the pre-PPS period, LTCHs were paid their (estimated) costs, up to facility-specic per-day
36:1368:111179:16:16:71:535:539:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:limit (MedPAC 2014). For most facilities, this limit was binding. Forthese facilities, we calculate
36:1369:111278:16:17:71:537:523:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the LTCH payment schedule as the per-day limit multiplied by the length of the stay. For a small
36:1370:111374:17:16:71:536:506:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:number of facilities, the payment limit does not appear to bind. For these facilities, we assume that
36:1371:111475:16:16:71:535:490:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:reported costs are linear in the patients length of stay, and we calculate the payment schedule as
36:1372:111574:16:16:71:535:474:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:the (imputed) per-day cost multiplied by the length of stay. When we analyze discharge patterns
36:1373:111669:16:17:71:536:458:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:in the pre-PPS period, we assign each stay the SSO threshold it would have had in the rst year
36:1374:111764:17:38:71:506:441:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:PPS period, based on the DRG assignments made using the CGS software described above.
36:1375:111849:38:27:71:288:403:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:Appendix C. Mortality analysis
36:1376:111879:27:16:71:534:376:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:We formally test for a mortality e¤ect using a regression discontinuity (RD) design. Letiindex
36:1377:111973:16:16:72:536:360:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:individuals andtindex days relative to the SSO threshold. Letyitbe a mortality indicator. For
36:1378:112066:16:16:71:534:344:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:our analysis of the 1-day mortality hazard,yittakes a value of1if the individual dies on daytand
36:1379:112162:16:17:71:535:328:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:takes on a value of0if the individual is alive. For the 30-day mortality analysis,yittakes on a
36:1380:112257:17:16:71:536:311:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:value of1if the individual dies in the subsequent 30 days and takes on a value of0if the individual
36:1381:112356:16:16:71:528:295:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:does not die over this period. Individuals who have already died are excluded from the analysis.
36:1382:112452:16:16:88:535:279:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In our baseline RD specication, we allow for a linear trend in the running variabletand permit
36:1383:112547:16:28:71:373:263:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:this linear trend to vary on di¤erent sides of the SSO threshold:
36:1384:112612:28:27:217:535:235:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:yit=-0+-1t+ 1t-0(\f0+\f1t) +"it.(13)
36:1385:112648:27:16:71:537:208:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:The coe¢cient of interest\f0captures the change in mortality at the SSO threshold, conditional
36:1386:112742:16:16:71:534:192:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on the linear controls. To conrm the robustness of our ndings, we also estimate a specication
36:1387:112838:16:17:71:531:176:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with a quadratic time trend that, as before, is also allowed to vary on di¤erent sides of the SSO
36:1388:112935:17:16:71:115:159:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:threshold:
36:1389:112945:16:23:184:535:143:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f3:yit=-0+-1t+-2t2+ 1t-0(\f0+\f1t+\f2t2) +"it:(14)
36:1390:112992:23:16:71:533:120:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:In both specications, we restrict our analysis to observations close thethreshold, focusing on
36:1391:113087:16:16:71:531:104:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:bandwidths of 3, 5, and 10 days within the threshold. We cluster our standard errors at the DRG
36:1392:113182:16:0:71:536:88:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:level, which allows for correlation in the health process not only within an individual over time but
37:1393:113285:0:17:71:534:708:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:also within the set of individuals who have the same DRG and therefore may exhibit correlated
37:1394:113378:17:16:71:428:691:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality proles. We focus the mortality analysis on the post-PPS period.
37:1395:113452:16:16:88:534:675:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Table A3 shows the parameter estimates with Panel A focusing on the 1-day mortality
37:1396:113544:16:16:71:534:659:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hazard. Column (1) of Panel A, which shows our baseline specication with alinear time trend
37:1397:113636:16:17:71:535:643:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and a 3-day bandwidth, indicates that 1-day mortality increases by less than 0.01 percentage point
37:1398:113734:17:16:71:534:626:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:at the threshold. This estimate is tiny in absolute magnitude, small relativeto the baseline daily
37:1399:113832:16:16:71:535:610:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:mortality rate of 0.6%, and is statistically indistinguishable from zero. Columns (2) to (6) show that
37:1400:113934:16:17:71:536:594:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:this nding is robust to alternative bandwidths and a quadratic time trend. Panel B shows e¤ects
37:1401:114030:17:16:71:534:577:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:on the 30-day mortality hazard. Column (1) of Panel B, which again shows the baseline specication
37:1402:114128:16:16:71:534:561:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:with a linear time trend and a 3-day bandwidth, indicates an economically tiny and statistically
37:1403:114224:16:16:71:535:545:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:insignicant 0.003 percentage point decline in 30-day mortality at the threshold (relative to a
37:1404:114319:16:17:71:536:529:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:baseline 30-day mortality rate of 13.5%). As before, the e¤ect is robust to alternative bandwidths
37:1405:114417:17:16:71:200:512:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:and a quadratic time trend.
37:1406:114444:16:16:88:536:496:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Because our standard errors in the regression discontinuity analysis rely on di¢cult-to-test
37:1407:114536:16:16:71:535:480:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:assumptions about the correlation structure of the error term, we assess the robustness of our
37:1408:114630:16:17:71:536:464:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:statistical inference using permutation inference (Rosenbaum 1984, 2002; Abadie et al.  2014).
37:1409:114724:17:16:71:536:447:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Specically, we estimate equation (13) with a bandwidth of 3, replacingthe dummy variable for
37:1410:114817:16:16:71:534:431:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:being to the right of the SSO threshold with a dummy variable for being to the right of placebo
37:1411:114912:16:16:71:534:415:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:thresholds dened att=-12andt= 42in the pre- and post-PPS periods. That is, we estimate
37:1412:114999:16:17:71:534:399:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:an RD e¤ect for a placebo threshold at each day starting 3 days after thestart of our sample and
37:1413:115095:17:16:71:535:382:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:ending 3 days before the end (to allow for a 3-day bandwidth); we alsoexclude days -3 to 3 in the
37:1414:115192:16:16:71:494:366:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:post-PPS period since these days might be contaminated by a potentialtreatment e¤ect.
37:1415:115277:16:16:88:535:350:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:Appendix Figure A4 plots the actual e¤ect and the distribution of placeboestimates for the
37:1416:115367:16:17:71:535:334:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:1-day and 30-day mortality hazards. The plots show that the actual change in mortality at the
37:1417:115460:17:16:71:534:317:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:SSO threshold is not particularly large relative to the typical day-to-day variation in the mortality
37:1418:115561:16:16:71:533:301:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hazard. The distributions of placebo estimates imply a p-value of 0.796 for the 1-day mortality
37:1419:115656:16:38:71:366:285:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:hazard and a p-value of 0.757 for the 30-day mortality hazard.
